
<span id="page-12-0"></span>Fig. 2. Security experiment  $\mathsf{Exp}^{\mathsf{X},\mathsf{Qc}}_{\mathsf{PRE},\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda})$  in the multi-challenge, multi-user setting. The adversary may adaptively issue any polynomial number of oracle queries. For the aHRA security, where  $\mathsf{X} = \mathsf{aHRA}$  we provide  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{aHRA}} = (\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{KGen}}, \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{CorrT}}, \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{CorrK}}, \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{Enc}}, \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{Chall},\mathsf{b}}, \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{ReEnc}})$ . For the aCCA security, where  $\mathsf{X} = \mathsf{aCCA}$  we additionally give the decryption oracle, i.e.,  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{aCCA}} = (\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{KGen}}, \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{CorrT}}, \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{CorrK}}, \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{Enc}}, \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{Dec}}, \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{Chall},\mathsf{b}}, \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{ReEnc}})$ . The parameter  $\mathsf{Qc}$  denotes the number of  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{Chall},\mathsf{b}}$  queries. The challenger implicitly maintains all key pairs  $(\mathsf{pk}_i, \mathsf{sk}_i)$  and all re-encryption tokens  $\mathsf{d}_{i,j}$ . Since the scheme is bi-directional,  $\mathsf{d}_{i,j}$  can be derived from  $\mathsf{d}_{j,i}$ .

- Setup( $1^{\lambda}$ ,  $\ell$ )  $\rightarrow$  (lpk,td): Takes a dimension  $\ell$  and a security parameter  $\lambda$  as input, and outputs a public key lpk and a trapdoor td. We denote by  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk} \subseteq \mathcal{X}$  the NP-language defined by lpk, and a relation  $\mathcal{R}_{lpk} \subseteq \mathcal{L}_{lpk} \times \mathcal{W}$ , where  $\mathcal{X}$  is a space associated with  $\ell$  and  $\mathcal{W}$  is the witness space. The relation  $\mathcal{R}_{lpk}$  specifies the valid statement-witness pairs for  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ .
- Sample<sub>L</sub>(lpk)  $\rightarrow$  (x,w): Takes the public key lpk as input, and outputs a uniformly random statement x  $\leftarrow$  U ( $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ ) and a witness w such that (x, w)  $\in$   $\mathcal{R}_{lpk}$ .
- $\mathsf{Sample}_{\mathcal{X}}(1^{\lambda}) \to \mathsf{x}$ : Takes the security parameter  $1^{\lambda}$  as input, and outputs a uniformly random statement  $\mathsf{x} \hookleftarrow \mathsf{U}(\mathcal{X})$  from the ambient space.
- CheckSMP<sub>L</sub>(lpk, td, x)  $\rightarrow$  {0,1}: Takes the public key lpk, trapdoor td, and a statement x as input, and outputs 0 or 1 depending on whether x is in the language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$  defined by lpk.

We require the following properties:

{13}------------------------------------------------

**Correctness:** For all security parameter  $\lambda$ , all (lpk, td)  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  Setup( $1^{\lambda}$ ,  $\ell$ ) generated by the setup algorithm, all statements  $x \in \mathcal{X}$ , we have  $x \in \mathcal{L}_{lpk}$  is equivalent to CheckSMP $_{\mathcal{L}}$ (lpk, td, x) = 1. Namely,

$$x \in \mathcal{L}_{lpk} \iff CheckSMP_{\mathcal{L}}(lpk, td, x) = 1.$$

**Hard Subset Membership:** For all security parameter  $\lambda$ , for all (lpk,td)  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  Setup( $1^{\lambda}$ ,  $\ell$ ) generated by the setup algorithm, the distribution of statements  $\times$  sampled from Sample\_ $\mathcal{L}$ (lpk) is computationally indistinguishable from the distribution of statements sampled from Sample\_ $\mathcal{L}$ ( $1^{\lambda}$ ). Formally, for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we have

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{HSM}}_{\mathsf{SMP},\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda}) = \left| \Pr \left[ b' = b \, \middle| \,$$

**Linearly Homomorphism:** For all security parameter  $\lambda$ , for all (lpk,td)  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  Setup( $1^{\lambda}$ ,  $\ell$ ) generated by the setup algorithm, the statement set  $\mathcal{X}$  forms a vector space with respect to a finite field  $\mathbb{F}$ , and the language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk} \subset \mathcal{X}$  is a subspace of  $\mathcal{X}$ . Moreover, we require that for all  $\alpha \in \mathbb{F}$ , and  $x_1, x_2 \in \mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ , the following holds:

$$(\mathsf{x}_1,\mathsf{w}_1) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{lpk}} \wedge (\mathsf{x}_2,\mathsf{w}_2) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{lpk}} \implies (\alpha \mathsf{x}_1 + \mathsf{x}_2, \alpha \mathsf{w}_1 + \mathsf{w}_2) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{lpk}}.$$

Language linearity: For all security parameter  $\lambda$ , for any two language public keys generated as  $(lpk, td) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} Setup(1^{\lambda}, \ell)$  and  $(lpk', td') \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} Setup(1^{\lambda}, \ell + n)$ , let  $\mathcal{X}$  and  $\mathcal{X}'$  be the statement spaces associated with dimensions  $\ell$  and  $\ell + n$  respectively, and let  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk} \subseteq \mathcal{X}$  and  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk'} \subseteq \mathcal{X}'$  be the corresponding languages. We require that  $\dim(\mathcal{L}_{lpk}) = \dim(\mathcal{L}_{lpk'}) = k$  where  $k = \dim(\mathcal{W})$ , and there exists a linear transformation matrix  $\mathbf{M} = (\mathbf{I}_{\ell}, \mathbf{r}_1, \dots, \mathbf{r}_n)^{\intercal} \in \mathbb{F}^{(\ell+n) \times \ell}$ , where each  $\mathbf{r}_i \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell}$  is sampled uniformly from the orthogonal complement of  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$  in  $\mathcal{X}$ , such that  $\mathbf{M}$  defines an isomorphism  $\mathbf{M} : \mathcal{L}_{lpk} \to \mathcal{L}_{lpk'}$  between the two languages. That is,  $\mathbf{M}(\mathcal{L}_{lpk}) = \mathcal{L}_{lpk'}$  and for all  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathcal{X}$ , we have

$$(x,w) \in \mathcal{R}_{lpk} \iff (\mathbf{M} \cdot x,w) \in \mathcal{R}_{lpk'}.$$

#### 4.2 Vector Space Hash Proof System

Let SMP be the subset membership language distribution of Definition 5, and let  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$  denote the language indexed by public key lpk. We now define the associated hash proof system UHPS for  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ .

**Definition 6 (Vector Space Hash Proof System).** A vector space hash proof system consists of four PPT algorithms UHPS = (Setup, PKGen, PEval, SEval) with the following syntax:

-  $\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda) \to \mathsf{hk}$ : Takes a security parameter  $\lambda$  as input, and outputs a hash  $key \; \mathsf{hk}$ .

{14}------------------------------------------------

- PKGen(hk,  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ )  $\rightarrow$  projk: Takes a hash key hk and a language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$  as input, and outputs a projection key projk.
- PEval(projk, x, w)  $\rightarrow$  hv: Takes a projection key projk, a statement x, and a witness w for x, and outputs a hash value hv.
- SEval(hk,x)  $\rightarrow$  hv: Takes a hash key hk and a statement x as input, and outputs a hash value hv.

Note that  $lpk \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} SMP.Setup(1^{\lambda}, \ell)$  is the public parameter output by the setup algorithm, which defines the language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ . We denote by  $\mathcal{R}_{lpk}$  the relation associated with  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ , which specifies the valid statement-witness pairs for  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ .

We require the following properties:

**Correctness:** For all security parameter  $\lambda$ , all hk  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  Setup(1 $^{\lambda}$ ), and all (x, w) such that w is a valid witness for x, it holds that

$$\Pr[\mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk}, \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}), \mathsf{x}, \mathsf{w}) = \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}, \mathsf{x})] = 1.$$

**Vector Space Structure:** Let  $\mathbb{F}$  denote a finite field. The sets of hash keys  $\mathcal{HK}$ , projection keys  $\mathcal{PK}_{lpk}$  (which determined by lpk), statements  $\mathcal{X}$ , and witnesses  $\mathcal{W}$  each form an  $\mathbb{F}$ -vector space. The hash value space  $\mathcal{HV} = \mathbb{F}$ . Also, we require that  $\dim(\mathcal{X}) = \dim(\mathcal{HK})$ 

**Homomorphism:** Due to the vector space structure, we require the following homomorphism

- Projection-Homomorphism: The projection keys satisfy the homomorphism property: For all security parameter  $\lambda$ , all  $\alpha \in \mathbb{F}$ , all  $\mathsf{hk}_1 \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$  and  $\mathsf{hk}_2 \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$ , it holds that

$$\mathsf{PKGen}(\alpha \cdot \mathsf{hk}_1 + \mathsf{hk}_2, \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}) = \alpha \cdot \mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk}_1, \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}) + \mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk}_2, \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}).$$

- Message-Homomorphism: For all security parameter  $\lambda$ , all  $\alpha \in \mathbb{F}$ , all hk  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  Setup(1 $^{\lambda}$ ), for all statements  $\mathsf{x}_1, \mathsf{x}_2 \in \mathcal{X}$ , it holds that

$$\begin{aligned} &\mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk},\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}),\alpha\cdot\mathsf{x}_1+\mathsf{x}_2,\alpha\cdot\mathsf{w}_1+\mathsf{w}_2) \\ &= \alpha\cdot\mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk},\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}),\mathsf{x}_1,\mathsf{w}_1) + \mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk},\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}),\mathsf{x}_2,\mathsf{w}_2). \end{aligned}$$

- Key-Homomorphism: For all security parameter  $\lambda$ , all  $\alpha \in \mathbb{F}$ , all  $\mathsf{hk}_1 \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$  and  $\mathsf{hk}_2 \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$ , for all statement  $\mathsf{x} \in \mathcal{X}$ , it holds that

$$SEval(\alpha \cdot hk_1 + hk_2, x) = \alpha \cdot SEval(hk_1, x) + SEval(hk_2, x).$$

**Key Collision:** Given the language parameter lpk, for any two different sampling of  $hk_1 \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} Setup(1^{\lambda})$  and  $hk_2 \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} Setup(1^{\lambda})$ , the probability that they produce the same projection key is negligible. Formally, we have

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{KCol}}_{\mathsf{UHPS},\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda}) := \Pr \left[ \mathsf{projk}_1 = \mathsf{projk}_2 \; \middle| \; \begin{aligned} \mathsf{hk}_1, \mathsf{hk}_2 & \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \\ \mathsf{projk}_1 = \mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk}_1, \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}) \\ \mathsf{projk}_2 = \mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk}_2, \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}) \end{aligned} \right] \\ &= \mathsf{negl}(\lambda). \end{split}$$

{15}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-15-2"></span> $(\varepsilon, d)$ -Universality: For any security parameter  $\lambda$ , let lpk  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  SMP.Setup $(1^{\lambda})$ , hk  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  Setup $(1^{\lambda})$ , and projk := PKGen(hk,  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk})$ . Given the projection key projk, the set of hash keys consistent with projk is defined as

$$\mathcal{HK}' := \{ \mathsf{hk}' \in \mathcal{HK} \mid \mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk}', \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}) = \mathsf{projk} \}.$$

We require that

- $-\mathcal{H}\mathcal{K}' \subseteq \mathcal{H}\mathcal{K}$  forms an affine  $\mathbb{F}$ -vector space of dimension d, we denote by  $\mathcal{H}\mathcal{K}''$  the vector space corresponded to the affine vector space  $\mathcal{H}\mathcal{K}'$ .
- Let x be a uniformly random statement from  $\mathcal{X}$ , then the probability that  $\mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk''},x) = 0$  for all  $\mathsf{hk''} \in \mathcal{HK''}$  is  $\varepsilon$ . Formally, we have

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{BadUniv}}_{\mathsf{VSHPS},\mathcal{A}}(1^\lambda) :=$$

$$\Pr \big[ \forall \mathsf{hk''} \in \mathcal{HK''} : \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk''}, \mathsf{x}) = 0 \ \big| \, \mathsf{x} \hookleftarrow \mathsf{U} \left( \mathcal{X} \right) \, \big] \leq \varepsilon.$$

We note that for any  $hk \in \mathcal{HK}''$  and any  $x \in \mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ , we have SEval(hk, x) = 0.

**d-MinEntropy** Following the definition of  $(\varepsilon, d)$ -universality, let  $\mathcal{HK}''$  be the d-dimensional vector space corresponding to the affine space  $\mathcal{HK}'$ . For any set of statements  $\{x_1, \ldots, x_n\} \subseteq \mathcal{X}$  (where  $n \geq d$ ) such that the linear span of their projections onto the dual space of  $\mathcal{HK}''$  has dimension exactly d, i.e.,

$$\dim(span\{\langle \mathsf{x}_i,\mathsf{hk''}\rangle : \mathsf{hk''} \in \mathcal{HK''}, i = 1,\ldots,n\}) = d,$$

the distribution of hash values

$$(\mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk''}, \mathsf{x}_1), \dots, \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk''}, \mathsf{x}_n)), \quad \mathsf{hk''} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{U}\left(\mathcal{HK''}\right)$$

has min-entropy exactly  $d \log_2 |\mathbb{F}|$ , due to the fact that only d out of the n hash values are truly independent. Specifically, for any  $\mathbf{y} \in \mathbb{F}^n$ , we have

$$\Pr [(\mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk''}, \mathsf{x}_1), \dots, \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk''}, \mathsf{x}_n)) = \mathbf{y}] \leq |\mathbb{F}|^{-d}.$$

## <span id="page-15-1"></span>5 aHRA-secure PRE with Tight Security

In this section, we present tightly aHRA-secure proxy re-encryption (PRE) schemes. Our constructions are based on the key-homomorphic properties of hash proof systems (HPS). Specifically, we adapt the HPS from the Cramer-Shoup cryptosystem [CS02] to build a PRE scheme. A limitation of this HPS is its single-use nature for elements outside the language, which inherently restricts the resulting PRE scheme to single-challenge security. To achieve multi-challenge security, we incorporate a ciphertext randomization technique from [HLG23].

This approach yields two constructions: a single-challenge scheme,  $\mathsf{PRE}_1$ , and a multi-challenge scheme,  $\mathsf{PRE}_2$ . Both can be described within a unified framework, differing only in the underlying HPS. For  $\mathsf{PRE}_1$ , we use a  $(\varepsilon, 1)$ -universal VSHPS. For  $\mathsf{PRE}_2$ , we employ a  $(\varepsilon, k)$ -universal VSHPS over a linear language of dimension k.

<span id="page-15-0"></span>The  $\mathcal{HK}''$  defines the secret keys that project to the zero projection key over the language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ . Thus, by perfect correctness and the key-homomorphism property, for any  $hk \in \mathcal{HK}''$  and any  $x \in \mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ , we have  $\mathsf{SEval}(hk, x) = 0$ .

{16}------------------------------------------------

## **5.1 Generic Construction from a Vector Space HPS**

We begin with a generic construction of an aHRA-secure PRE scheme from a vector space hash proof system (VSHPS). The construction is detailed in Figure [3.](#page-16-0)

```
Alg Setup(1λ
              ):
01 (lpk,td) ←$ SMP.Setup(1λ
                              , ℓ)
02 return pp := lpk
Alg KGen(pp):
03 hk ←$ VSHPS.Setup(1λ
                           )
04 projk := PKGen(hk, Llpk)
05 pk := projk; sk := hk
06 return (pk,sk)
Alg Enc(pk,
             -
              m

                 ):
07 (x, w) ←$ SampleL(lpk)
08 ct0 := x
09 ct1 := PEval(pk, x, w) + m
10 return ct := (ct0, ct1)
                                               Alg RKGen(ski,skj ):
                                               11 parse hki =: ski; hkj =: skj
                                               12 di,j := hkj − hki
                                               13 return di,j
                                               Alg REnc(di,j , pki
                                                                  , pkj
                                                                       , cti):
                                               14 parse (cti,0, cti,1) =: cti; xi =: cti,0
                                               15 (x
                                                     ′
                                                     , w
                                                        ′
                                                         ) ←$ SampleL(lpk)
                                               16 ct0 := xi + x
                                                                ′
                                               17 ct1 := cti,1 + SEval(di,j , xi)
                                                                    +PEval(pkj
                                                                                 , x
                                                                                   ′
                                                                                    , w
                                                                                      ′
                                                                                       )
                                               18 return ct := (ct0, ct1)
                                               Alg Dec(sk, ct):
                                               19 m := ct1 − SEval(sk, ct0)
                                               20 return m
```

<span id="page-16-0"></span>**Fig. 3.** Construction of aHRA-secure proxy re-encryption PRE with tight security from vector space hash proof system (VSHPS).

The correctness of the construction in Figure [3](#page-16-0) is established by verifying the decryption of both original and re-encrypted ciphertexts. This relies on the correctness and linear properties of the underlying vector space hash proof system (VSHPS).

**Correctness of Decryption.** For a fresh ciphertext ct := Enc(pk*,* m), where ct = (x*,* PEval(pk*,* x*,*w) + m), decryption with the corresponding secret key sk works as follows:

$$\begin{aligned} \mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct}) &= \mathsf{ct}_1 - \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct}_0) \\ &= (\mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{x},\mathsf{w}) + \mathsf{m}) - \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{x}) = \mathsf{m}. \end{aligned}$$

The final step holds due to the correctness property of the VSHPS, which guarantees that PEval(pk*,* x*,*w) = SEval(sk*,* x) for any statement x in the language.

**Correctness of Re-encryption.** Let ct*<sup>i</sup>* be a ciphertext under key pk*<sup>i</sup>* , and let ct*<sup>j</sup>* := REnc(d*i,j ,* pk*<sup>i</sup> ,* pk*<sup>j</sup> ,* ct*i*) be the re-encrypted ciphertext. Decrypting ct*<sup>j</sup>*

{17}------------------------------------------------

with secret key  $sk_i$  yields:

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{sk}_j, \mathsf{ct}_j) &= \mathsf{ct}_{j,1} - \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{sk}_j, \mathsf{ct}_{j,0}) \\ &= \left(\mathsf{ct}_{i,1} + \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{d}_{i,j}, \mathsf{x}_i) + \mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{pk}_j, \mathsf{x}', \mathsf{w}')\right) - \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{sk}_j, \mathsf{x}_i + \mathsf{x}') \\ &= \left(\mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{pk}_i, \mathsf{x}_i, \mathsf{w}_i) + \mathsf{m} + \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_j - \mathsf{hk}_i, \mathsf{x}_i) \right. \\ &\quad + \mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{pk}_j, \mathsf{x}', \mathsf{w}')\right) - \left(\mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_j, \mathsf{x}_i) + \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_j, \mathsf{x}')\right) \\ &= \left(\mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_i, \mathsf{x}_i) + \mathsf{m} + \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_j, \mathsf{x}_i) - \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_i, \mathsf{x}_i) \right. \\ &\quad + \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_j, \mathsf{x}')\right) - \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_j, \mathsf{x}_i) - \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_j, \mathsf{x}') \\ &= \mathsf{m}. \end{split}$$

This demonstrates that the original message is recovered after re-encryption and subsequent decryption. The derivation uses the linearity of SEval, the definition of the re-encryption key  $d_{i,j} = hk_j - hk_i$ , and the correctness property of the VSHPS. In Section 5.2 and Section 5.3, we prove that the construction from Figure 3 achieves tight aHRA security in both single- and multi-challenge settings, depending on the universality of the underlying VSHPS. Specifically:

- An  $(\varepsilon, 1)$ -universal VSHPS yields a scheme with tight single-challenge aHRA security.
- An  $(\varepsilon, d)$ -universal VSHPS over a k-dimensional linear language, where  $d \ge k$ , yields a scheme with tight multi-challenge aHRA security.

#### <span id="page-17-0"></span>5.2 Single-challenge aHRA security

<span id="page-17-1"></span>**Theorem 1.** Let VSHPS be a  $(\varepsilon, 1)$ -universal vector space hash proof system, the proxy re-encryption scheme PRE described in Figure 3 is SC-aHRA secure. Formally, we have:

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{SC}\text{-}\mathsf{aHRA}}_{\mathsf{PRE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq \binom{\mathsf{N}_{\mathsf{U}}}{2} \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{KCol}}_{\mathsf{SMP},\mathcal{B}}(1^{\lambda}) + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{HSM}}_{\mathsf{SMP},\mathcal{B}}(1^{\lambda}) + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{BadUniv}}_{\mathsf{VSHPS},\mathcal{B}}(1^{\lambda}).$$

*Proof.* We prove the above theorem via the following hybrid security games  $\mathbf{G}_0, \ldots, \mathbf{G}_5$ . The detailed security games are presented in Figure 7.

<u>Game  $G_0$ </u>: This is the original SC-aHRA security game. By definition, the adversary's success probability is

$$\mathsf{pr}_0 \coloneqq \left| \Pr \Big[ \mathsf{Exp}^{\mathsf{SC-aHRA}}_{\mathsf{PRE},\mathcal{A}} (1^{\lambda}) = 1 \Big] - \frac{1}{2} \right| = 1.$$

Game  $G_1$ : This game is identical to  $G_0$ , except that the challenger aborts if a collision occurs among the generated public keys. Let  $\mathsf{Bad}_{\mathsf{Col}}$  be the event that there exist distinct  $i, j \in [\mathsf{N}_{\mathsf{U}}]$  such that  $\mathsf{pk}_i = \mathsf{pk}_j$ . If  $\mathsf{Bad}_{\mathsf{Col}}$  occurs, the game aborts.

{18}------------------------------------------------

The difference in the adversary's success probability between these two games is bounded by the probability of the event  $\mathsf{Bad}_\mathsf{Col}$ . Using a standard union bound argument, we have:

$$|\mathsf{pr}_0 - \mathsf{pr}_1| \leq \Pr[\mathsf{Bad}_\mathsf{Col}] \leq \binom{\mathsf{N}_\mathsf{U}}{2} \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{KCol}}_\mathsf{SMP,\mathcal{B}}(1^\lambda).$$

<u>Game G<sub>2</sub></u>: The only difference between  $G_2$  and  $G_1$  is that, the challenge ciphertext is encrypted using the hash key hk instead of using the public key projk. We generate the challenge ciphertext as

$$(\mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{w}) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Sample}_{\mathcal{L}}(\mathsf{lpk}) \qquad \mathsf{ct}_1 := \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}, \mathsf{ct}_0) + \mathsf{m}_b$$

since  $ct_0 \in \mathcal{L}_{lpk}$  with witness w, by the perfect correctness of HPS, the challenge ciphertext in  $G_2$  and  $G_1$  is identical. Therefore, we have

$$pr_2 = pr_1$$
.

Game  $G_3$ : In this game, we change the way the challenge ciphertext is generated. Instead of sampling  $\mathsf{ct}_0$  from the language using  $\mathsf{Sample}_{\mathcal{L}}(\mathsf{lpk})$ , we sample it from the statement set  $\mathsf{ct}_0 \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Sample}_{\mathcal{X}}(1^\lambda)$ . Since the bad event  $\mathsf{Bad}_{\mathsf{Col}}$  does not occur, the adversary cannot simply check if  $\mathsf{ct}_0 \in \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}$  by checking if  $\mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_i - \mathsf{hk}_j, \mathsf{ct}_0) = 0$  where i, j are any two collision users. <sup>13</sup> By the hard subset membership property of the language, we have

$$|\mathsf{pr}_3 - \mathsf{pr}_2| \leq \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{SMP},\mathcal{B}}^{\mathsf{HSM}}(1^{\lambda}).$$

Game  $G_4$ : In  $G_4$ , we introduce a bad event  $Bad_{Univ}$  that captures the failure of the universal hash property. Specifically, we define

$$\mathsf{Bad}_{\mathsf{Univ}} \coloneqq \{ \forall \mathsf{hk}'' \in \mathcal{HK}'' : \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}'', \mathsf{x}) = 0 \mid \mathsf{ct}_0 \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{U}(\mathcal{X}) \}.$$

We recall that  $\mathcal{HK}' \subset \mathcal{HK}$  is the affine vector space, such that we can change hk to any element  $hk' \in \mathcal{HK}'$  without changing its projection over  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ .  $\mathcal{HK}''$  is the vector space that corresponds to the affine vector space  $\mathcal{HK}'$ . Using the universality of VSHPS, we have

$$|\mathsf{pr}_4 - \mathsf{pr}_3| \leq \Pr[\mathsf{Bad}_{\mathsf{Univ}}] \leq \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Bad}\mathsf{Univ}}_{\mathsf{VSHPS},\mathcal{B}}(1^\lambda).$$

Game  $G_5$ : In this game, we modify the generation of the challenge ciphertext. We sample  $v \leftarrow U(\mathbb{F})$  at the beginning of the experiment and compute the

<span id="page-18-0"></span>If there is a collision between two public keys  $\mathsf{pk}_i$  and  $\mathsf{pk}_j$ , then the adversary can simply gets  $\mathsf{hk}_i - \mathsf{hk}_j \in \mathcal{HK}''$  by querying the oracle  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{CorrT}}(i,j)$ , and check if  $\mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_i - \mathsf{hk}_j, \mathsf{ct}_0) = 0$ . If yes, then  $\mathsf{ct}_0 \in \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}$ , otherwise  $\mathsf{ct}_0 \notin \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}$ .

{19}------------------------------------------------

challenge ciphertext as  $\mathsf{ct}_1 := \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_i + v \cdot \mathsf{h}, \mathsf{ct}_0) + \mathsf{m}_b = \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_i, \mathsf{ct}_0) + \mathsf{m}_b + v \cdot \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{h}, \mathsf{ct}_0)$ , where i indexes the challenge user and  $\mathsf{h}$  is a basis of the  $\mathcal{HK}''$  with dimension d = 1 over the linear language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}$ .

We first give the value of  $|pr_5 - pr_4|$ , then we explain why the above modification is valid in Lemma 1. We have

$$|\mathsf{pr}_5 - \mathsf{pr}_4| = 0$$

In  $G_5$ , since the game does not abort, we are guaranteed that  $\mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{h},\mathsf{ct}_0) \neq 0$ . As v is chosen uniformly at random from  $\mathbb{F}$ , the term  $v \cdot \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{h},\mathsf{ct}_0)$  acts as a one-time pad, perfectly masking the message  $\mathsf{m}_b$ . Consequently, the adversary's advantage is zero, and its success probability is exactly 1/2. Thus, we have

$$\operatorname{pr}_5 = \frac{1}{2}.$$

<span id="page-19-0"></span>Taking union bound over all the hybrid games, we complete the proof.  $\Box$ 

**Lemma 1.** Games  $G_4$  and  $G_5$  are perfectly indistinguishable. That is, for any PPT adaptive adversary A, we have

$$\left| \Pr \left[ \mathbf{G}_{5}^{\mathcal{A}} \Rightarrow 1 \right] - \Pr \left[ \mathbf{G}_{4}^{\mathcal{A}} \Rightarrow 1 \right] \right| = 0,$$

where  $\Pr[G_i^A \Rightarrow 1]$  denotes the probability that adversary A outputs 1 in game  $G_i$ .

*Proof.* We prove the lemma via a reduction from adaptive to selective security. The argument is structured in two parts, formalized in Lemma 2 and Lemma 3.

Lemma 2 establishes that no PPT selective adversary can distinguish between  $G_4$  and  $G_5$  with any advantage. Subsequently, by Lemma 3, we can extend the result to any PPT adaptive adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  that no PPT adaptive adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  can distinguish between  $G_4$  and  $G_5$  with any advantage which completes the proof.

<span id="page-19-1"></span>**Lemma 2.** For any PPT selective adversary  $\mathcal{B}$ , which by definition needs to commit to all its oracle queries before the game begins, we have:

$$\left| \Pr \left[ \mathbf{G}_{5}^{\mathcal{B}} \Rightarrow 1 \right] - \Pr \left[ \mathbf{G}_{4}^{\mathcal{B}} \Rightarrow 1 \right] \right| = 0.$$

*Proof.* We prove the lemma by introducing three intermediate games,  $\mathbf{G}_{4.1}$ ,  $\mathbf{G}_{4.2}$  and  $\mathbf{G}_{4.3}$ , and show that for any selective adversary  $\mathcal{B}$ , the following chain of perfect indistinguishability holds:  $\mathbf{G}_4 \approx_p \mathbf{G}_{4.1} \approx_p \mathbf{G}_{4.2} \approx_p \mathbf{G}_{4.3} \approx_p \mathbf{G}_5$ . This implies that  $\mathbf{G}_4$  and  $\mathbf{G}_5$  are perfectly indistinguishable for  $\mathcal{B}$ .

The simulation strategy for these games, detailed in Figure 8, leverages the selective nature of  $\mathcal{B}$ . Since all oracle queries are known beforehand, the challenger can simulate the environment without knowing most secret keys corresponding to challenge queries. Without loss of generality, we re-index users such that all

{20}------------------------------------------------

challenge queries in  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{C}}$  are for users  $i \in \{1, \dots, |\mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{CL}}|\}$ .

Game  $G_{4.1}$ : This game, detailed in Figure 8, is identical to  $G_4$  but uses a different simulation strategy. The challenger generates a secret key  $hk_1$  and its corresponding public key  $projk_1$ . For other challenged users  $i \in \{2, ..., |V_{CL}|\}$ , public keys are defined as  $projk_i := projk_1 + PKGen(d_i, \mathcal{L}_{lpk})$  for randomly sampled  $d_i \in \mathcal{HK}$ . Keys for non-challenged users  $j > |V_{CL}|$  are generated normally. This setup allows for a perfect simulation of all oracle queries. Re-encryption and corruption queries involving only challenged users can be answered using the relative tokens, while those involving non-challenged users can be answered using their known secret keys. The only secret key needed for the challenge part of the simulation is  $hk_1$ , used to generate the challenge ciphertext. The distribution of all public keys and re-encryption keys is identical to that in  $G_4$ , so the games are perfectly indistinguishable. Thus, we have

$$pr_{4.1} = pr_4$$
.

Game  $G_{4.2}$ : This game modifies the generation of  $hk_1$ . Let  $h \in \mathcal{HK}''$  be a hash key such that  $SEval(h, ct_0) \neq 0$  for the challenge statement  $ct_0$ . Such an h is guaranteed to exist by the  $(\varepsilon, 1)$ -universality of the VSHPS. The challenger now computes  $hk_1 := hk_1' + v \cdot h$ , where  $hk_1' \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{HK}$  and  $v \leftarrow U(\mathbb{F})$ .

Since  $\mathcal{HK}$  is a vector space over  $\mathbb{F}$ , the distribution of  $\mathsf{hk}_1$  remains uniform over  $\mathcal{HK}$ . Furthermore, by the definition of  $\mathcal{HK}''$ , we have  $\mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk}_1, \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}) = \mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk}_1', \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}})$ . Thus, the adversary's view is identical to that in  $\mathbf{G}_{4.1}$ , and we have

$$pr_{4.2} = pr_{4.1}$$
.

Game  $G_{4.3}$ : This game modifies the generation of the projection key  $projk_1$  by using the hash key  $hk'_1$  instead of  $hk_1$ . Moreover, we answer all the oracle queries except  $\mathcal{O}_{Chall}$  using  $hk'_1$  instead of  $hk_1$ .

By the definition of  $\mathcal{HK}''$ , we have  $\mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk}_1, \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}) = \mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk}_1', \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}})$ , so the public key  $\mathsf{projk}_1$  remains unchanged. Since the adversary cannot query  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{CorrK}}(i)$  for user  $i \in [|\mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{CL}}|]$ ,  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{CorrK}}$  leaks no information about  $\mathsf{hk}_1$  or  $\mathsf{hk}_1'$ . When answering  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{CorrT}}(i,j)$  queries for challenged users  $i,j \in [|\mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{CL}}|]$ , we return  $\mathsf{d}_{i,j} = \mathsf{d}_j - \mathsf{d}_i^{14}$ , which is independent of both  $\mathsf{hk}_1$  and  $\mathsf{hk}_1'$ . For non-challenged users  $i,j > |\mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{CL}}|$ , we answer  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{CorrT}}(i,j)$  queries using their known secret keys, which are also independent of both  $\mathsf{hk}_1$  and  $\mathsf{hk}_1'$ .

As for  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{REnc}}(i,j)$ : if  $i,j \in [|\mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{CL}}|]$ , we compute the re-encryption ciphertexts using  $\mathsf{d}_{i,j} = \mathsf{d}_j - \mathsf{d}_i$ , which is independent of both  $\mathsf{hk}_1$  and  $\mathsf{hk}_1'$ ; if  $i \in [|\mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{CL}}|]$  but  $j > |\mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{CL}}|]$ , since  $(\mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{ct}_1) \in \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{HC}}$ , the only component  $\mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_i - \mathsf{hk}_1, \mathsf{ct}_0)$  that includes  $\mathsf{hk}_1$  is equal to  $\mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_i - \mathsf{hk}_1', \mathsf{ct}_0)$ . If  $i, j > |\mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{CL}}|$ , we compute the

<span id="page-20-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>14</sup> Assumes that  $d_1 = 0$ 

<span id="page-20-1"></span>SEval(hk<sub>i</sub> - hk<sub>1</sub>, ct<sub>0</sub>) = SEval(hk<sub>i</sub> - (hk'<sub>1</sub> +  $v \cdot h$ ), ct<sub>0</sub>) = SEval(hk<sub>i</sub> - hk'<sub>1</sub>, ct<sub>0</sub>) -  $v \cdot SEval(h, ct_0)$ . Since (ct<sub>0</sub>, ct<sub>1</sub>)  $\in \mathcal{L}_{HC}$ , we have SEval(h, ct<sub>0</sub>) = 0, thus SEval(hk<sub>i</sub> - hk'<sub>1</sub>, ct<sub>0</sub>) = SEval(hk<sub>i</sub> - hk<sub>1</sub>, ct<sub>0</sub>).

{21}------------------------------------------------

ciphertexts using their secret keys, which are also independent of both  $hk_1$  and  $hk_1'$ .

Therefore, the adversary's view in  $G_{4.2}$  and  $G_{4.3}$  is identical, leading to

$$pr_{4.3} = pr_{4.2}$$
.

In  $G_{4.3}$ , the challenge ciphertext component  $ct_1$  is computed as:  $ct_1 := SEval(hk'_1 + v \cdot h, ct_0) + m_b$ . The challenge ciphertext in  $G_5$  is computed as  $ct_1 := SEval(hk_1 + v \cdot h, ct_0) + m_b$ , where  $hk_1 \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{HK}$ . Since  $hk'_1$  and  $hk_1$  are both uniformly random in  $\mathcal{HK}$  and v is uniformly random in  $\mathbb{F}$ , the expressions  $hk'_1 + v \cdot h$  and  $hk_1 + v \cdot h$  follow the same distribution. Thus,  $G_{4.3}$  and  $G_5$  have identically distributed challenge ciphertexts.

The only difference between  $\mathbf{G}_{4.3}$  and  $\mathbf{G}_5$  lies in the generation of secret keys for users  $i \in \{2, \dots, |V_{\mathsf{CL}}|\}$ . In  $\mathbf{G}_{4.3}$ , these keys are implicitly defined as  $\mathsf{hk}_i = \mathsf{hk}_1 + \mathsf{d}_i$ , while in  $\mathbf{G}_5$ , they are sampled uniformly from  $\mathcal{HK}$ . However, since  $\mathsf{d}_i$  is uniformly random in  $\mathcal{HK}$  and independent of  $\mathsf{hk}_1$ , the distribution of  $\mathsf{hk}_i = \mathsf{hk}_1 + \mathsf{d}_i$  is uniform over  $\mathcal{HK}$  (by the vector space structure of  $\mathcal{HK}$ ).

Thus, the secret keys in both games are identically distributed, we have

$$\operatorname{pr}_5 = \operatorname{pr}_{4.3}$$

Combining the above results, we complete the proof.

<span id="page-21-0"></span>**Lemma 3 (S2A - Core Lemma).** For any two games  $G_1$  and  $G_2$ , if for any PPT selective adversary  $\mathcal{B}$ , we have

$$\left| \Pr \left[ \boldsymbol{G}_{2}^{\mathcal{B}} \Rightarrow 1 \right] - \Pr \left[ \boldsymbol{G}_{1}^{\mathcal{B}} \Rightarrow 1 \right] \right| = 0,$$

then for any PPT adaptive adversary A, we also have

$$\left| \Pr \left[ \mathbf{G}_{2}^{\mathcal{A}} \Rightarrow 1 \right] - \Pr \left[ \mathbf{G}_{1}^{\mathcal{A}} \Rightarrow 1 \right] \right| = 0.$$

*Proof.* We prove the lemma by contradiction. Assume, for the sake of contradiction, that there exists a PPT adaptive adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  that distinguishes between  $\mathbf{G}_1$  and  $\mathbf{G}_2$  with a non-zero advantage  $\varepsilon > 0$ . We show that this implies the existence of a PPT selective adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  that also distinguishes between the games with a non-zero advantage. This contradicts the premise of the lemma, which states that no such selective adversary exists.

The core of the argument is a standard reduction that converts the adaptive adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  into a selective adversary  $\mathcal{B}$ . The reduction relies on a query-guessing technique.

Let Q be a polynomial bound on the number of oracle queries made by  $\mathcal{A}$ . The selective adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  operates as follows:

1. Before the game begins,  $\mathcal{B}$  generates a guess  $\mathcal{Q}^*$  for the entire sequence of oracle queries that  $\mathcal{A}$  will make. Since  $\mathcal{A}$  is a PPT algorithm, the number of possible query sequences is at most exponential, and  $\mathcal{B}$  can guess the correct sequence with a non-zero, though possibly negligible, probability  $\gamma > 0$ .

{22}------------------------------------------------

- 2.  $\mathcal{B}$  commits to the guessed query sequence  $\mathcal{Q}^{\star}$  as required for a selective adversary.
- 3.  $\mathcal{B}$  then runs  $\mathcal{A}$  as a subroutine. When  $\mathcal{A}$  makes an oracle query,  $\mathcal{B}$  checks if it matches the next query in the pre-committed sequence  $\mathcal{Q}^*$ .
  - If the query matches,  $\mathcal{B}$  forwards it to its own challenger and returns the response to  $\mathcal{A}$ .
  - If the query does not match, or if  $\mathcal{A}$  makes more queries than predicted,  $\mathcal{B}$  aborts the simulation and outputs a random bit.
- 4. When  $\mathcal{A}$  terminates and outputs a bit b',  $\mathcal{B}$  outputs the same bit b'.

The adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  is a valid PPT selective adversary. If its initial guess  $\mathcal{Q}^*$  is correct (an event that occurs with probability  $\gamma$ ), then  $\mathcal{B}$  provides a perfect simulation of the environment for  $\mathcal{A}$ . In this scenario,  $\mathcal{A}$ 's distinguishing advantage is fully preserved. The overall distinguishing advantage of  $\mathcal{B}$  is therefore:

$$\left| \Pr \left[ \mathbf{G}_{2}^{\mathcal{B}} \Rightarrow 1 \right] - \Pr \left[ \mathbf{G}_{1}^{\mathcal{B}} \Rightarrow 1 \right] \right| = \gamma \cdot \left| \Pr \left[ \mathbf{G}_{2}^{\mathcal{A}} \Rightarrow 1 \right] - \Pr \left[ \mathbf{G}_{1}^{\mathcal{A}} \Rightarrow 1 \right] \right|$$

$$= \gamma \cdot \varepsilon.$$

Since both  $\gamma$  and  $\varepsilon$  are non-zero, their product is also non-zero. This means  $\mathcal{B}$  can distinguish between  $\mathbf{G}_1$  and  $\mathbf{G}_2$  with a non-zero advantage, which contradicts our initial assumption. Therefore, no such adaptive adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  can exist, and the lemma holds.

#### <span id="page-22-0"></span>5.3 Multi-challenge aHRA security

<span id="page-22-1"></span>**Theorem 2.** Let VSHPS be a  $(\varepsilon, d)$ -universal vector space hash proof system over a linear language of dimension k with dimension  $\ell \geq 2k$  ambient vector space  $\mathcal{X}$ , the proxy re-encryption scheme PRE described in Figure 3 is aHRA secure in the multi-challenge setting. Specifically, for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  making at most  $Q_{\mathsf{C}}$  challenge queries, there exists a PPT algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  such that  $\mathbf{T}(\mathcal{B}) \approx \mathbf{T}(\mathcal{A})$ , and we have:

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{aCCA}, \mathsf{Q_C}}_{\mathsf{PRE}_2, \mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq \binom{\mathsf{N_U}}{2} \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{KCol}}_{\mathsf{SMP}, \mathcal{B}}(1^\lambda) + 3 \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{HSM}, \mathsf{Q_C}}_{\mathsf{SMP}, \mathcal{B}}(1^\lambda) + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{BadUniv}}_{\mathsf{VSHPS}, \mathcal{B}}(1^\lambda).$$

*Proof.* The proof proceeds via a sequence of hybrid games,  $\mathbf{G}_0, \ldots, \mathbf{G}_9$ . The transitions from  $\mathbf{G}_0$  to  $\mathbf{G}_5$  mirror those in the proof of Theorem 1, but are adapted for the multi-challenge setting. The key differences are as follows:

- The transition from  $G_2$  to  $G_3$  relies on the  $Q_C$ -Hard Subset Membership ( $Q_C$ -HSM) property. All  $Q_C$  challenge statements are switched from being sampled in the language via  $\mathsf{Sample}_{\mathcal{L}}(\mathsf{lpk})$  to being sampled uniformly from the ambient space via  $\mathsf{Sample}_{\mathcal{L}}(1^{\lambda})$ . This introduces a difference of at most  $\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{SMP},\mathcal{B}}^{\mathsf{HSM},Q_C}(1^{\lambda})$ .
- In  $G_4$ , the bad event  $\mathsf{Bad}_{\mathsf{Univ}}$  is defined with respect to the  $(\varepsilon, d)$ -universality of the VSHPS. The difference is bounded by  $|\mathsf{pr}_4 \mathsf{pr}_3| \leq \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{VSHPS},\mathcal{B}}^{\mathsf{Bad}\mathsf{Univ}}(1^{\lambda})$ .

{23}------------------------------------------------

- In  $G_5$ , the challenge ciphertexts are randomized. Let  $\mathbf{H} \in \mathbb{F}^{d \times \ell}$  be a basis of the  $\mathcal{HK}''$  over linear language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}$ . We sample a random vector  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}^d$  and compute all the challenge ciphertexts as  $\mathsf{ct}_1^j := \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_i + \mathbf{v}^\intercal \cdot \mathbf{H}, \mathsf{ct}_0^j) + \mathsf{m}_b^j$  for  $j \in [\mathsf{Q}_\mathsf{C}]$  and  $i \in [\mathsf{N}_\mathsf{U}]$  indexing the challenge users. The proof of this step is similar to the single-challenge case, but requires a more detailed explanation.
  - In  $G_{4.1}$ , we index the first challenge user as 1 and generate its secret key  $hk_1$  normally. We add a relative token  $d_i$  for each other challenge user i, <sup>17</sup> so we can still simulate all the oracle queries perfectly without knowing any other user's secret key in list  $V_{CL}$ .
  - In  $\mathbf{G}_{4.2}$  and  $\mathbf{G}_{4.3}$ , we do the same modification as in the single-challenge case, except that we now sample a random vector  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}^d$  and compute  $\mathsf{hk}_1 := \mathsf{hk}_1' + \mathbf{v}^\intercal \cdot \mathbf{H}$ , where  $\mathsf{hk}_1' \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{HK}$ . We note that since all the challenge users share the same hash key  $\mathsf{hk}_1$ , the random vector  $\mathbf{v}^\intercal \mathbf{H}$  is also shared among all challenge ciphertexts.

As in the single-challenge case, this change is purely conceptual, so  $pr_5 = pr_4$ .

The subsequent games,  $G_6$  through  $G_9$ , are detailed in Figure 9 and complete the reduction.

Game  $G_6$ : In this game, we change the distribution of the statement component  $\mathsf{ct}_0$  for all challenge ciphertexts. Instead of sampling  $\mathsf{ct}_0$  uniformly from the ambient space via  $\mathsf{Sample}_{\mathcal{L}}(1^\lambda)$ , we sample it from the language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}_1}$  via  $\mathsf{Sample}_{\mathcal{L}}(\mathsf{lpk}_1)$ , where  $\mathsf{lpk}_1 \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{SMP}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda,\ell)$  is new language public key. The difference between  $G_5$  and  $G_6$  is bounded by the  $\mathsf{Q}_\mathsf{C}\text{-HSM}$  property of the language.

$$|\mathsf{pr}_6 - \mathsf{pr}_5| \leq \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{SMP},\mathcal{B}}^{\mathsf{HSM},\mathsf{Qc}}(1^{\lambda}).$$

Game  $G_7$ : This game is a conceptual rewrite of  $G_6$ . We first express the challenge ciphertext generation in matrix form, which does not change the adversary's view. Then we rewrite the term  $\mathsf{SEval}(\mathbf{v}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{H},\mathsf{x})$  as  $\mathbf{R}^\mathsf{T} \cdot \mathsf{x}$  where  $\mathbf{R}^\mathsf{T} := \mathsf{SEval}(\mathbf{v}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{H},\mathbf{u}_1)|\cdots|\mathsf{SEval}(\mathbf{v}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{H},\mathbf{u}_\ell)$  is a row vector in  $\mathbb{F}^{1\times\ell}$  and  $\mathbf{u}_1,\ldots,\mathbf{u}_\ell$  is the standard basis of  $\mathbb{F}^\ell$ . Since  $\mathsf{SEval}$  is linear in its second argument, we have  $\mathsf{SEval}(\mathbf{v}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{H},\mathsf{x}) = \mathbf{R}^\mathsf{T} \cdot \mathsf{x}$ . Finally, we modify the vector  $\mathbf{R}$  to be uniformly random in a subspace of dimension d of  $\mathbb{F}^{\ell\times 1}$ , where d is the dimension of the hash key space  $\mathcal{HK}''$ . This change is valid because  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}^d$  is uniformly random,  $\mathbf{H} \in \mathbb{F}^{d\times\ell}$  has full row rank d, and the vectors  $\mathbf{u}_1,\ldots,\mathbf{u}_\ell$  are linearly independent. By the d-MinEntropy property of VSHPS,  $\mathbf{R}$  has full min-entropy  $d \cdot \log |\mathbb{F}|$ . Thus we can rewrite  $\mathbf{R}$  as  $\mathbf{r}^\mathsf{T}\mathbf{M} + \mathbf{R}_0$  for some fixed  $\mathbf{R}_0 \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell\times 1}$ , where  $\mathbf{r} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{U}(\mathbb{F}^d)$  and  $\mathbf{M} \in \mathbb{F}^{d\times\ell}$  is a full row rank matrix, the change is purely conceptual.

Thus, the games are perfectly indistinguishable.

$$pr_7 = pr_6$$
.

<span id="page-23-0"></span>We note that there are at most  $Q_C$  challenge ciphertexts which are distributed among  $N_U$  users and the exact allocation of ciphertexts to users is not fixed.

<span id="page-23-1"></span>The adversary may not query a  $\mathcal{O}_{CorrT}$  between two challenge users, which means the graph of  $V_{CL}$  is not connected.

{24}------------------------------------------------

Game  $G_8$ : This game reinterprets the structure of the challenge ciphertext. In  $G_7$ , the vector added to the core encryption is  $\begin{pmatrix} \mathsf{x} \\ \mathsf{r}^\intercal \mathbf{M} \cdot \mathsf{x} \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{I}_\ell \\ \mathsf{r}^\intercal \mathbf{M} \end{pmatrix} \cdot \mathsf{x}$ . Since  $\mathbf{I}_\ell$  is the  $\ell \times \ell$  identity matrix,  $\mathbf{r}$  is a random vector in  $\mathbb{F}^d$  with  $d \geq k = \dim(\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}})$  and  $\mathbf{M} \in \mathbb{F}^{d \times \ell}$  is a full row rank matrix, the matrix  $\begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{I}_\ell \\ \mathsf{r}^\intercal \mathbf{M} \end{pmatrix}$  is an isomorphism from  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}_1}$  to a new linear space of dimension k over an ambient space of dimension  $\ell+1$ . We denote this new linear space as  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}_1'}$ , and rewrite the challenge ciphertexts as  $\begin{pmatrix} \mathsf{ct}_0 \\ \mathsf{ct}_1 \end{pmatrix} := \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{0} \\ \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_i, \bar{\mathsf{x}'}) + \mathsf{m}_b + \mathbf{R}_0^\intercal \cdot \bar{\mathsf{x}'} \end{pmatrix} + \mathsf{x'}$ , where  $(\mathsf{x'}, \mathsf{w}) \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Sample}_{\mathcal{L}}(\mathsf{lpk}_1')$  and  $\bar{\mathsf{x}'}$  is the first  $\ell$  components of  $\mathsf{x'}$ .

This change is purely conceptual, thus, we have

$$pr_8 = pr_7$$
.

**Game G**<sub>9</sub>: In this final game, we change how x' is generated for all challenge ciphertexts. Instead of sampling x' from the language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk'_1}$  via  $\mathsf{Sample}_{\mathcal{L}}(lpk'_1)$ , we sample it uniformly at random from the ambient space  $\mathbb{F}^{\ell+1}$  via  $\mathsf{Sample}_{\mathcal{L}}(1^{\lambda})$ . The indistinguishability between  $\mathbf{G}_8$  and  $\mathbf{G}_9$  is guaranteed by the  $\mathsf{Q}_{\mathsf{C}}$ -HSM property of the language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk'}$ . <sup>18</sup>

$$|\mathsf{pr}_9 - \mathsf{pr}_8| \leq \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{SMP},\mathcal{B}}^{\mathsf{HSM},\mathsf{Q}_\mathsf{C}}(1^{\lambda}).$$

In  $G_9$ , the challenge ciphertext is computed as  $\begin{pmatrix} \mathsf{ct}_0 \\ \mathsf{ct}_1 \end{pmatrix} := \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{0} \\ \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_i, \mathsf{x}') + \mathsf{m}_b \end{pmatrix} + \mathsf{x}'$ , where  $\mathsf{x}'$  is a uniformly random vector in  $\mathbb{F}^{\ell+1}$ . This vector acts as a one-time pad, making the resulting ciphertext  $(\mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{ct}_1)$  uniformly random and statistically independent of the challenge bit b. Consequently, the adversary's advantage is zero.

$$\operatorname{pr}_9 = \frac{1}{2}.$$

Combining the above results, we complete the proof.

#### 5.4 Instantiation with MDDH assumption

We give the instantiation of the subset membership language and the vector space hash proof system based on the MDDH assumption in bilinear groups.

<span id="page-24-0"></span>The language dimension remains the same, but the ambient space dimension differs (from  $\mathbb{F}^{\ell}$  to  $\mathbb{F}^{\ell+1}$ ), which causes a negligible difference in the HSM advantage. For simplicity, we uniformly denote it by  $\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{SMP},\mathcal{B}}^{\mathsf{HSM},\mathsf{Qc}}(1^{\lambda})$ .

{25}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-25-1"></span>**Subset Membership Language** Let  $\mathbb{G}$  be a prime order group of order p, and let g be a generator of  $\mathbb{G}$ . We define a language public key as  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{G}^{\ell \times k}$ , where k is an arbitrary integer and  $\ell \geq 2k$  varies dependent on the properties we need. More specifically, for  $(\varepsilon, d)$ -universal VSHPS instantiation, we will need  $d = \ell - k \geq k$ . We give the full description of subset membership language in Figure 4, where  $(\mathbb{G}, p, g) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{GGen}(1^{\lambda})$  generates a DDH hard group. The

```
\begin{array}{lll} & & & & & & & & & & & & & & & & & &
```

<span id="page-25-0"></span>**Fig. 4.** Instantiation of the subset membership language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$  based on the MDDH assumption in bilinear groups. Here,  $\left[\cdot\right]_1$  denotes the group action that maps elements from  $\mathbb{Z}_p$  to  $\mathbb{G}$ . Base(ker A) denotes the probabilistic algorithm that returns a basis for the kernel of the matrix A.

properties of this construction are established as follows.

**Correctness** The correctness of the membership check follows from the definition of the language and the trapdoor. For any statement  $\mathbf{x} = \left[\mathbf{A}\right]_1 \mathbf{r}$  in the language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ , we have  $\mathbf{K}^\intercal \mathbf{x} = \mathbf{K}^\intercal (\left[\mathbf{A}\right]_1 \mathbf{r}) = \mathbf{0}$ , since  $\mathbf{K}$  is a basis for the left kernel of  $\mathbf{A}$ .

**Hard Subset Membership** The indistinguishability of a random statement  $x \in \mathbb{G}^{\ell}$  from a statement in the language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$  is guaranteed by the Matrix Decisional Diffie-Hellman (MDDH) assumption. Moreover, due to the random self-reducibility [EHK<sup>+</sup>13], we have

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{MDDH},\mathsf{Q}_{\mathbb{G}}}_{\mathbb{G},\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda}) = \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{MDDH}}_{\mathbb{G},\mathcal{B}}(1^{\lambda}) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

**Linear Homomorphism** The language is a k-dimensional vector space over  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ , and thus is linearly homomorphic by construction.

Language linearity This property is established as follows: Given two language public keys  $lpk = [\mathbf{A}]_1$  and  $lpk' = [\mathbf{A}']_1$ , where  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell \times k}$  and  $\mathbf{A}' \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\ell+n) \times k}$  are both full-rank matrices, the languages  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk} = \{[\mathbf{A}]_1 \mathbf{w} : \mathbf{w} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^k\}$  and  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk'} = \{[\mathbf{A}']_1 \mathbf{w} : \mathbf{w} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^k\}$  are isomorphic as k-dimensional vector spaces. The linear transformation matrix  $\mathbf{M} = (\mathbf{I}_\ell, \mathbf{r}_1, \dots, \mathbf{r}_n)^\intercal \in \mathbb{F}^{(\ell+n) \times \ell}$ , where each  $\mathbf{r}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_p^\ell$  is sampled uniformly from the orthogonal complement of span( $\mathbf{A}$ ) in  $\mathbb{Z}_p^\ell$ , defines the isomorphism  $\mathbf{M} : \mathcal{L}_{lpk} \to \mathcal{L}_{lpk'}$ . Specifically, for

{26}------------------------------------------------

any  $x = [A]_1 w \in \mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ , we have

$$\mathbf{M} \cdot \mathbf{x} = \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{I}_{\ell} \\ \mathbf{r}_{1}^{\mathsf{T}} \\ \vdots \\ \mathbf{r}_{n}^{\mathsf{T}} \end{pmatrix} \cdot \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{A} \end{bmatrix}_{1} \mathbf{w} = \begin{pmatrix} \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{A} \end{bmatrix}_{1} \\ \mathbf{r}_{1}^{\mathsf{T}} \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{A} \end{bmatrix}_{1} \\ \vdots \\ \mathbf{r}_{n}^{\mathsf{T}} \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{A} \end{bmatrix}_{1} \end{pmatrix} \mathbf{w} = \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{A}' \end{bmatrix}_{1} \mathbf{w} \in \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}'}$$

where  $\mathbf{A}' = (\mathbf{A}^{\mathsf{T}}, \mathbf{A}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{r}_1, \dots, \mathbf{A}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{r}_n)^{\mathsf{T}} \in \mathbb{Z}_{\mathsf{p}}^{(\ell+n)\times k}$  maintains full rank k, ensuring that  $\mathbf{M}(\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}) = \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}'}$  and the relation is preserved:  $(\mathsf{x}, \mathsf{w}) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{lpk}} \iff (\mathbf{M} \cdot \mathsf{x}, \mathsf{w}) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{lpk}'}$ .

**Vector Space Hash Proof System** With respect to the subset membership language defined in Figure 4, we give the instantiation of the vector space hash proof system in Figure 5 based on the MDDH assumption. The properties of this

```
\begin{array}{ll} \underline{\mathbf{Alg}\;\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda):} \\ \hline 01\;\;\mathsf{hk} := \mathbf{k} \hookleftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_p^\ell\right) \\ 02\;\;\mathbf{return}\;\mathsf{hk} \\ \hline \\ \underline{\mathbf{Alg}\;\mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{projk},\mathsf{x},\mathsf{w}):} \\ 06\;\;\mathbf{parse}\;\mathbf{k}^\mathsf{T}\left[\mathbf{A}\right]_1 =: \mathsf{projk};\;\mathbf{w} =: \mathsf{w} \\ 07\;\;\mathbf{return}\;\mathsf{hv} := \left(\mathbf{k}^\mathsf{T}\left[\mathbf{A}\right]_1\right) \cdot \mathbf{w} \\ \hline \\ \underline{\mathbf{Alg}\;\mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk},\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}):} \\ 03\;\;\mathbf{parse}\;\mathbf{k} =: \mathsf{hk} \\ 04\;\;\mathsf{projk} := \mathbf{k}^\mathsf{T}\left[\mathbf{A}\right]_1 \\ 05\;\;\mathbf{return}\;\mathsf{projk} \\ \hline \end{array}
```

<span id="page-26-0"></span>**Fig. 5.** Construction of VSHPS with respect to the subset membership language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk} = Span(\mathbf{A})$ .

construction are established as follows.

**Correctness** The correctness follows from the definitions of PEval and SEval. For any statement  $x \in \mathcal{L}_{lpk}$  with witness w, we have  $PEval(projk, x, w) = (\mathbf{k}^{\intercal} [\mathbf{A}]_1) \cdot w = \mathbf{k}^{\intercal} \cdot ([\mathbf{A}]_1 w) = SEval(hk, x)$ .

Vector Space Structure Let  $\mathbb{F} = \mathbb{Z}_p$ , the vector space structure is preserved in the projection, as the projection of a linear combination of vectors is the same linear combination of their projections.

**Homomorphism** The projection operation is a homomorphism, meaning it respects the linear structure of the space.

**Key Collision Resistance** Given two distinct secret keys  $\mathbf{k}, \mathbf{k}' \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell}$ , the probability that  $\mathbf{k}^{\mathsf{T}} \left[ \mathbf{A} \right]_1 = \mathbf{k}'^{\mathsf{T}} \left[ \mathbf{A} \right]_1$  is negligible. This would require  $\mathbf{k} - \mathbf{k}'$  to lie in the left kernel of  $\left[ \mathbf{A} \right]_1$ . Since  $\mathbf{k} - \mathbf{k}'$  is non-zero and uniformly random, the probability is at most  $1/\left| \mathbb{F} \right|^k$ , which is negligible.

 $(\varepsilon, d)$ -Universality The construction is  $(\varepsilon, d)$ -universal given a projection key projk :=  $\mathbf{k}^{\intercal} [\mathbf{A}]_1$ , since all public evaluations are of the form  $\mathbf{k}^{\intercal} [\mathbf{A}]_1 \cdot \mathbf{r}$ . Therefore,  $\mathbf{k} + \mathsf{Span}(\ker([\mathbf{A}]_1))$  forms an affine vector space of dimension  $d = \ell - k$  consistent with all public evaluations.

{27}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-27-0"></span>d-MinEntropy We have SEval(hk",x) = hk"·x where hk"  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  U ( $\mathcal{HK}''$ ) and  $\mathcal{HK}''$  is d-dimensional. For any n statements  $\{x_1, \ldots, x_n\}$  (where  $n \geq d$ ) with exactly d linearly independent among them, the hash values (hk"· $x_1, \ldots, hk$ "· $x_n$ ) have min-entropy  $d \log_2 |\mathbb{Z}_p|$  because the d-dimensional hk" can extract d independent values through inner products.

## <span id="page-27-1"></span>6 CCA-secure PRE with (almost) tight security

In this section, we extend the aHRA-secure proxy re-encryption scheme from Figure 3 to achieve CCA-security with a tight security reduction.

# 6.1 General construction of aCCA construction with (almost) tight security

Our construction builds upon the aHRA-secure proxy re-encryption scheme from Section 5. In that scheme, a ciphertext  $\mathsf{ct} = (\mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{ct}_1)$  consists of a statement  $\mathsf{ct}_0$  for a hard subset membership problem and a component  $\mathsf{ct}_1 = \mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{projk}_i, \mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{w}) + \mathsf{m}$  that binds the message  $\mathsf{m}$  to an evaluation of  $\mathsf{ct}_0$  within a hash proof system.

The first obstacle in attaining CCA2-security is that it forbids any ciphertext malleability. A standard remedy, going back to [CHK03], is to append a one-time signature to the ciphertext. One might be tempted to sign the component ct<sub>1</sub>, since it binds the message. However, re-encryption necessarily modifies ct<sub>1</sub>, thereby invalidating the signature. This conflict precludes a direct extension of the aHRA-secure scheme to a aCCA-secure construction.

To resolve this issue, we restructure the ciphertext by introducing a third component,  $\mathsf{ct}_2$ . We redefine  $\mathsf{ct}_1$  to be solely the hash proof system evaluation, i.e.,  $\mathsf{ct}_1 = \mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{projk}_i, \mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{w})$ , and move the message to  $\mathsf{ct}_2$ , setting  $\mathsf{ct}_2 = \mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{projk}, \mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{w}) + \mathsf{m}$ , where  $\mathsf{projk}$  is a public parameter common to all users. Consequently,  $\mathsf{ct}_2$  remains invariant under re-encryption, which only affects  $\mathsf{ct}_1$ . This design allows us to sign the pair  $(\mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{ct}_2)$  to ensure integrity, providing the non-malleability required for aCCA-security while preserving the re-encryption functionality.

Furthermore, inspired by the Cramer-Shoup cryptosystem, we must ensure that the ciphertext components  $ct_0$  and  $ct_1$  are well-formed. Specifically, we need to prove that  $ct_1$  is a valid evaluation of  $ct_0$  under the hash proof system. We achieve this by incorporating a simulation-sound tag-based language-malleable NIZK proof system (tLM-NIZK). This system proves the relation between  $ct_0$  and  $ct_1$  using a tag derived from the one-time signature's verification key, i.e., t = H(ovk). The final ciphertext is thus  $ct = (ct_0, ct_1, ct_2, ovk, \sigma, \pi)$ .

Remark 1. It is important to note that our NIZK proof system cannot directly prove that  $\mathsf{ct}_1$  is a valid evaluation of  $\mathsf{ct}_0$  under the hash proof system in general, since the hash function may not possess the necessary linear structure required by the Groth-Sahai proof system [GS08]. Fortunately, the specific hash proof system

{28}------------------------------------------------

instantiated from the MDDH assumption that we employ does exhibit such linear structure, making our construction viable. Whether there exists a NIZK proof system capable of proving this relation for arbitrary hash proof systems remains an open question.

Our construction combines the following cryptographic primitives:

- A language distribution SMP for a subset membership problem.
- A vector space hash proof system VSHPS.
- A one-time signature scheme OTS.
- A special simulation-sound tag-based language-malleable NIZK proof system tLM-NIZK for the language sampled by SMP.

We give our construction in Figure 6.

```
Alg Setup(1^{\lambda}):
                                                                                     Alg Enc(pk_i, m):
01 (lpk, td) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} SMP.Setup(1^{\lambda}, \ell)
                                                                                     12 (x, w) \leftarrow Sample_{\mathcal{C}}(lpk)
02 projk \leftarrow U (\mathcal{PK}_{lpk})
                                                                                     13 ct_0 := x
03 crs \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi. Setup(1^{\lambda})
                                                                                    14 ct_1 := PEval(projk_i, x, w)
                                                                                     15 ct_2 := PEval(projk, x, w) + m
04 \text{ return pp} := (lpk, projk, crs)
                                                                                     16 (ovk, osk) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} OTS.KGen(1^{\lambda})
Alg KGen(pp):
                                                                                     17 t = H(ovk)

\overline{05 \text{ sk}_i := \mathsf{hk}'_i} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{VSHPS.Setup}(1^{\lambda})

                                                                                     18 \sigma \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{OTS}.\mathsf{Sig}(\mathsf{osk},(\mathsf{ct}_0,\mathsf{ct}_2))
06 \mathsf{projk}_i' := \mathsf{PKGen}(\mathsf{hk}_i', \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}})
                                                                                     19 \pi \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbf{\Pi}.\mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{t},(\mathsf{ct}_0,\mathsf{ct}_1),\mathsf{w})
07 pk_i := projk_i = projk + projk'_i
                                                                                     20 return ct := (\mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{ct}_1, \mathsf{ct}_2, \mathsf{ovk}, \sigma, \pi)
08 return (pk_i, sk_i)
                                                                                     Alg Dec(sk, ct):
Alg RKGen(sk_i, sk_j):
                                                                                    21 parse sk_i := hk'_i; t = H(ovk)
\overline{09 \ \mathbf{parse} \ \mathsf{sk}_i \coloneqq \mathsf{hk}_i';} \ \mathsf{sk}_j \coloneqq \mathsf{hk}_j'
                                                                                     22 check \Pi. Ver(crs, t, (ct<sub>0</sub>, ct<sub>1</sub>), \pi) = 1
10 \mathsf{d}_{i,j} := \mathsf{hk}_j' - \mathsf{hk}_i'
                                                                                     23 check OTS.Ver(ovk, (ct<sub>0</sub>, ct<sub>2</sub>), \sigma) = 1
11 return d_{i,j}
                                                                                     24 return m := ct_2 - ct_1 + SEval(sk_i, ct_0)
                                                                                     Alg REnc(d_{i,j}, pk<sub>i</sub>, pk<sub>j</sub>, ct):
                                                                                    25 parse (\mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{ct}_1, \mathsf{ct}_2, \mathsf{ovk}, \sigma, \pi) \coloneqq \mathsf{ct}
                                                                                     26 t = H(ovk)
                                                                                     27 check \Pi.Ver(crs, t, (ct<sub>0</sub>, ct<sub>1</sub>), \pi) = 1
                                                                                    28 \operatorname{ct}_1' := \operatorname{ct}_1 + \operatorname{\mathsf{SEval}}(\mathsf{d}_{i,j},\operatorname{\mathsf{ct}}_0)
                                                                                    29 \pi' \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbf{\Pi}.\mathsf{Trans}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{lpk}_i,\mathsf{t},\mathsf{ct}_0,\pi,\mathsf{d}_{i,j})
                                                                                    30 return ct := (\mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{ct}_1', \mathsf{ct}_2, \sigma, \pi')
```

<span id="page-28-0"></span>**Fig. 6.** General construction of aCCA-secure PRE scheme  $PRE_{aCCA}$  with (almost) tight security.  $\Pi$  is a tLM-NIZK of the statement  $\exists w : x \in_{w} \mathcal{L}_{lpk} \land ct_1 = PEval(projk_i, x, w)$ .

{29}------------------------------------------------

**Correctness.** The correctness of the above construction follows from the correctness of the underlying VSHPS, OTS, and tLM-NIZK proof system.

```
\begin{split} &\mathsf{ct}_2 - \mathsf{ct}_1 + \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{sk}_i, \mathsf{ct}_0) \\ =& \mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{projk}, \mathsf{x}, \mathsf{w}) + \mathsf{m} - \mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{projk}_i, \mathsf{x}, \mathsf{w}) + \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{sk}_i, \mathsf{ct}_0) \\ =& \mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{projk}, \mathsf{x}, \mathsf{w}) + \mathsf{m} - \mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{projk} + \mathsf{projk}_i', \mathsf{x}, \mathsf{w}) + \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{sk}_i, \mathsf{ct}_0) + \mathsf{m} \\ =& \mathsf{m} - \mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{projk}_i', \mathsf{x}, \mathsf{w}) + \mathsf{SEval}(\mathsf{hk}_i', \mathsf{x}) = \mathsf{m}. \end{split}
```

#### 6.2 (Almost) Tight aCCA security

The security proof for our aCCA-secure scheme builds upon the proof for the aHRA-secure scheme from Section 5. However, handling the decryption and re-encryption oracles requires additional arguments.

**Decryption Oracle Queries** We now detail the handling of decryption oracle queries. The core of the argument relies on the unforgeability of the one-time signature scheme and the special simulation-soundness of the tLM-NIZK proof system. When the oracle receives a ciphertext  $\mathsf{ct} = (\mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{ct}_1, \mathsf{ct}_2, \mathsf{ovk}, \sigma, \pi)$  for decryption under a key  $\mathsf{pk}_i$ , we distinguish between the following cases. We denote components of the challenge ciphertext with a superscript star  $(\star)$ .

The query uses the challenge verification key  $ovk^*$  but is not a re-encryption of the challenge ciphertext. This case covers queries where  $ovk = ovk^*$  but either  $\sigma \neq \sigma^*$  or  $(ct_0, ct_2) \neq (ct_0^*, ct_2^*)$ . For the decryption query to be valid,  $\sigma$  must be a valid signature on  $(ct_0, ct_2)$  under  $ovk^*$ . This constitutes a forgery against the one-time signature scheme, an event that occurs with only negligible probability due to its unforgeability.

A collision has been found for ovk. This corresponds to the case where  $ovk \neq ovk^*$ , but  $t = H(ovk) = H(ovk^*) = t^*$ . If this event occurs, the adversary has successfully found a collision for the hash function.

The query uses a new verification key. This is the case where  $ovk \neq ovk^*$ . The challenger has not simulated a proof for the tag t = H(ovk). By the special simulation-soundness of the tLM-NIZK system, an adversary cannot produce a valid proof  $\pi$  for a false statement. Thus, if the proof verifies, it must be that  $\exists w : x \in_{\mathsf{w}} \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}} \wedge \mathsf{ct}_1 = \mathsf{PEval}(\mathsf{projk}_i, \mathsf{x}, \mathsf{w})$ . The information revealed by such a query does not compromise the  $(\varepsilon, d)$ -universality of the hash proof system, as the set of possible hash keys consistent with all public information and oracle queries remains an affine subspace of dimension at least d.

The query is a re-encryption of the challenge ciphertext. This corresponds to the case where  $\mathsf{ovk} = \mathsf{ovk}^\star$ ,  $\sigma = \sigma^\star$ , and  $(\mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{ct}_2) = (\mathsf{ct}_0^\star, \mathsf{ct}_2^\star)$ . Here, the query ciphertext  $\mathsf{ct}$  differs from the challenge ciphertext  $\mathsf{ct}^\star$  only in the components  $\mathsf{ct}_1$  and  $\pi$ . For the proof  $\pi$  to be valid, the special simulation-soundness of the tLM-NIZK implies that  $\mathsf{ct}$  must be a valid re-encryption of some ciphertext. Since the other components match the challenge,  $\mathsf{ct}$  must be a re-encryption of  $\mathsf{ct}^\star$ . Such queries are disallowed by the definition of CCA-security, so the oracle can reject them.

{30}------------------------------------------------

**Re-Encryption Oracle Queries** The primary difference in handling reencryption oracle queries compared to the aHRA-secure scheme arises when the adversary requests the re-encryption of a self-generated ciphertext from an uncorrupted user to a corrupted user. In other scenarios, the simulation can rely on the re-encryption key, which is available since re-encryption key is either for two uncorrupted users or for two corrupted users.

To handle the challenging case, we add a check to the algorithm REnc which ensures the components ct<sup>0</sup> and ct<sup>1</sup> is well-formed, i.e., **Π***.*Ver(crs*,*t*,*(ct0*,* ct1)*, π*) = 1. This ensures that only well-formed ciphertexts—those for which there exists a witness w such that x ∈<sup>w</sup> Llpk and ct<sup>1</sup> = PEval(projk*<sup>i</sup> ,* x*,*w)—can be re-encrypted. If this check fails, the oracle rejects the query. In this case, all the information the adversary can learn from OReEnc is through the evaluation of SEval(d*i,j ,* ct0) for users *i* and *j*, which leak no information except the public keys. [19](#page-30-0)

Summarizing the above intuitions, we have the following theorem.

**Theorem 3 (**aCCA **security).** *Let* PREaCCA *be the proxy re-encryption scheme from Figure [6.](#page-28-0) Assume that*

- **–** SMP *is a hard subset membership language distribution.*
- **–** *The vector space hash proof system* VSHPS *is* (*ε, d*)*-universal for negligible ε with d* ≥ 1 *for single-challenge* aCCA *or d* ≥ *k for multi-challenge* aCCA*.*
- **–** *The one-time signature scheme* OTS *is strongly unforgeable.*
- **–** *The* tLM*-*NIZK *proof system* **Π** *is a (one-time/unbounded) extractable special simulation-sound tag-based language-malleable* NIZK *proof system.*

*Then,* PREaCCA *is* aCCA*-secure. We will prove the theorem in [G](#page-62-0) and [H.](#page-68-0)*

<span id="page-30-0"></span><sup>19</sup> We will prove this result in the security proof of aCCA-security PRE.

{31}------------------------------------------------
