## A Assumptions

We first describe the Matrix Diffie-Hellman assumption [EHK<sup>+</sup>17].

**Definition 7** ( $(\ell, k)$ -Full Rank Matrix Distribution). Let  $(\ell, k) \in \mathbb{N}$  be integers with  $\ell > k$ . We define the  $(\ell, k)$ -full rank matrix distribution  $\mathcal{D}_{\ell, k}$  uniformly outputs  $\mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell \times k}$  matrices, where the first k rows forms a full-rank matrix in  $\mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times k}$ .

For simplicity of notation, we denote the distribution by  $\mathcal{D}_k = \mathcal{D}_{k+1,k}$  when  $\ell = k+1$ .

For a group  $\mathbb{G}$  of prime order p, we give the MDDH assumption over  $\mathbb{G}$ . Note that in pairing groups  $(\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2, \mathbb{G}_T)$ , variants of MDDH assumption can be defined over  $\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2$  respectively.

**Definition 8** ( $\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k}$ -MDDH **Assumption).** The  $\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k}$ -MDDH assumption holds over  $\mathbb{G}$ , if for all PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , the following advantage  $\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{MDDH}}_{\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k},\mathbb{G},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda)$  is negligible.

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{MDDH}}_{\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k},\mathbb{G},\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda}) := \left| \Pr \left[ \mathcal{A}(\left[\mathbf{A}\right]_{1}, \left[\mathbf{u}\right]_{1}) = 1 \right] - \Pr \left[ \mathcal{A}(\left[\mathbf{A}\right]_{1}, \left[\mathbf{Aw}\right]_{1}) = 1 \right] \right|.$$

where 
$$\mathbf{A} \hookleftarrow \mathcal{D}_{\ell,k}$$
,  $\mathbf{w} \hookleftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_{\mathsf{p}}^{k}\right)$ , and  $\mathbf{u} \hookleftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_{\mathsf{p}}^{\ell}\right)$ .

Similarly, we define the  $\mathcal{U}_{\ell,k}$ -MDDH assumption over  $\mathbb{G}$  as same as  $\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k}$ -MDDH assumption except that  $\mathbf{A} \leftarrow \mathcal{U}_{\ell,k}$ . Then we give two lemmas which show the relationship between  $\mathcal{D}$ -MDDH and  $\mathcal{U}$ -MDDH assumptions.

For an integer  $Q \geq 1$ , we define the Q- $\mathcal{D}$ -MDDH problem over  $\mathbb{G}$  as follows.

**Definition 9** (Q- $\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k}$ -MDDH). The Q- $\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k}$ -MDDH assumption holds over  $\mathbb{G}$ , if for all PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , the following advantage  $\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k},\mathbb{G},\mathcal{A}}^{Q$ -MDDH}( $\lambda$ ) is negligible.

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{Q\text{-MDDH}}_{\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k},\mathbb{G},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) := \left| \Pr \big[ \mathcal{A}( \left[ \mathbf{A} \right]_1, \left[ \mathbf{AW} \right]_1) = 1 \right] - \Pr \big[ \mathcal{A}( \left[ \mathbf{A} \right]_1, \left[ \mathbf{U} \right]_1) = 1 \right] \right|.$$

where 
$$\mathbf{A} \hookleftarrow \mathcal{D}_{\ell,k}$$
,  $\mathbf{W} \hookleftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_{\mathsf{p}}^{k \times Q}\right)$ , and  $\mathbf{U} \hookleftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_{\mathsf{p}}^{\ell \times Q}\right)$ .

 $\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k}$ -MDDH problem is random self-reducible [EHK<sup>+</sup>13], i.e., Q- $\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k}$ -MDDH and  $\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k}$ -MDDH problems can be tightly reduced to each other. Especially, if the distribution is change to  $\mathcal{U}_{\ell,k}$ , the reduction is even tighter.

Lemma 4 (Random Self-Reducibility of MDDH [EHK+13]). Let  $Q \ge \ell - k$ , for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  such that for all security parameter  $\lambda$ , the following holds.

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{Q\operatorname{\mathsf{-MDDH}}}_{\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k},\mathbb{G},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq (\ell-k) \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{MDDH}}_{\mathcal{D}_{\ell,k},\mathbb{G},\mathcal{B}}(\lambda) + \frac{1}{p-1}.$$

Let  $Q \ge \ell - k$ , for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  such that for all security parameter  $\lambda$ , the following holds.

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{Q\operatorname{\mathsf{-MDDH}}}_{\mathcal{U}_{\ell,k},\mathbb{G},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{MDDH}}_{\mathcal{U}_{\ell,k},\mathbb{G},\mathcal{B}}(\lambda) + \tfrac{1}{p-1}.$$

{34}------------------------------------------------

**Definition 10 (Signature Scheme).** A signature scheme  $\Sigma = (\mathsf{KGen}, \mathsf{Sig}, \mathsf{Ver})$  with the following syntax.

- $\mathsf{KGen}(1^{\lambda}) \to (\mathsf{ssk}, \mathsf{svk})$ : The key generation algorithm takes as input a security parameter  $\lambda$  and outputs a signing key  $\mathsf{ssk}$  and a verification key  $\mathsf{svk}$ .
- $Sig(ssk, m) \rightarrow \sigma$ : The signing algorithm takes as input a signing key ssk and a message m, and outputs a signature  $\sigma$ .
- $Ver(svk, m, \sigma) \rightarrow \{0, 1\}$ : The verification algorithm takes as input a verification key svk, a message m, and a signature  $\sigma$ , and outputs 1 if the signature is valid, and 0 otherwise.

We require the following properties:

One-Time Unforgeable: For any PPT adversary A, the probability of successfully forging a signature on a new message after seeing one valid signature is negligible. Formally, for any A, we have:

$$\Pr \left[ \mathsf{Ver}(\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{m}',\sigma') = 1 \land \mathsf{m}' \neq \mathsf{m} \; \middle| \; \begin{matrix} (\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{ssk}) := \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^\lambda), \\ \mathsf{m} := \mathcal{M}, \sigma := \mathsf{Sig}(\mathsf{ssk},\mathsf{m}), \\ (\mathsf{m}',\sigma') := \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{m},\sigma) \end{matrix} \right] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

where  $\mathcal{M}$  is the message space and  $\operatorname{negl}(\lambda)$  denotes a negligible function in  $\lambda$ . Unforgeability against Chosen Message Attacks: For any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , the probability of successfully forging a signature on a new message after querying the signing oracle is negligible. Formally, for any  $\mathcal{A}$ , we have:

$$\Pr\left[ \begin{array}{c} \mathsf{Ver}(\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{m}',\sigma') = 1 \\ \land \; \mathsf{m}' \notin \{m_1,\ldots,m_q\} \end{array} \middle| \begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{ssk}) := \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^\lambda), \\ (\mathsf{m}_1,\sigma_1),\ldots,(\mathsf{m}_q,\sigma_q) \\ := \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{Sig}(\mathsf{ssk},\cdot)}(\mathcal{A}(\mathsf{svk})), \\ (\mathsf{m}',\sigma') := \mathcal{A}^{\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{Sig}(\mathsf{ssk},\cdot)}}(\mathsf{svk}) \end{array} \right] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

where  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{Sig}}(\mathsf{ssk},\cdot)$  is the signing oracle,  $\mathcal{M}$  is the message space, and  $\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  denotes a negligible function in  $\lambda$ .

**Definition 11 (Commitment Scheme).** A commitment scheme Com is a tuple Com = (Setup, Com,) with the following syntax.

- Setup $(1^{\lambda}) \rightarrow \mathsf{ck}$ : Takes the security parameter as input, and outputs  $\mathsf{ck}$ .
- $Com(ck, m; open) \rightarrow c$ : Takes a commitment key ck and a message m, and an opening value open, and outputs a commitment c.

We require the following properties:

Hiding: For any security parameter  $\lambda$ , any two messages  $m_0$  and  $m_1$ , the commitments to these messages are indistinguishable. Formally, for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we have:

$$\left| \Pr \left[ b = b' \middle| \begin{array}{l} \mathsf{ck} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \\ (\mathsf{m}_0, \mathsf{m}_1) \xleftarrow{\$} \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{ck}) \\ b \hookleftarrow \{0, 1\}; \ \mathsf{open} \hookleftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathcal{O}\right) \\ \mathsf{c} := \mathsf{Com}(\mathsf{ck}, \mathsf{m}_b; \mathsf{open}) \\ b' := \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{ck}, \mathsf{c}) \end{array} \right] - \tfrac{1}{2} \right| \le \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

{35}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-35-0"></span>Binding: For any security parameter  $1^{\lambda}$ , it is infeasible to find two different messages  $m_0$  and  $m_1$  and corresponding opening values  $open_0$  and  $open_1$  such that they produce the same commitment. Formally, for any probabilistic polynomial-time adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we have:

$$\Pr \begin{bmatrix} \mathsf{m}_0 \neq \mathsf{m}_1 \land \\ \mathsf{Commit}(\mathsf{ck}, \mathsf{m}_0; \mathsf{open}_0) \\ = \mathsf{Commit}(\mathsf{ck}, \mathsf{m}_1; \mathsf{open}_1) \end{bmatrix} \begin{array}{c} \mathsf{ck} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda) \\ (\mathsf{c}, \mathsf{m}_0, \mathsf{m}_1, \mathsf{open}_0, \mathsf{open}_1) := \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{pp}) \end{bmatrix} \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

where  $negl(\lambda)$  denotes a negligible function in  $\lambda$ .

## B Groth-Sahai Non-interactive Zero-Knowledge Proofs

Groth and Sahai [GS08] introduced a unified methodology for constructing non-interactive witness-indistinguishable (NIWI) and non-interactive zero-knowledge (NIZK) proofs over bilinear groups  $\mathcal{PG}$ , capable of proving satisfiability for systems of mixed-type equations. The equations could be written in a unified way as:

$$\sum_{j=1}^{n} f(a_j, y_j) + \sum_{i=1}^{m} f(x_i, b_i) + \sum_{i=1}^{m} \sum_{j=1}^{n} f(x_i, \gamma_{ij} y_j) = t$$

where  $A_1, A_2, A_T$  are  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ -modules,  $\mathbf{x} \in A_1^m$ ,  $\mathbf{y} \in A_2^n$  are the variables,  $\mathbf{a} \in A_1^n$ ,  $\mathbf{b} \in A_2^m$ ,  $\Gamma = (\gamma_{ij}) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{m \times n}$ ,  $t \in A_T$  are the constants and  $f : A_1 \times A_2 \to A_T$  is bilinear map.

In order to simplify notation, we define:

$$\mathbf{x} \cdot \mathbf{y} = \sum_{i=1}^{n} f(x_i, y_i).$$

The equations can now be written as:

$$\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{y} + \mathbf{x} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{x} \cdot \Gamma \mathbf{y} = t.$$

Because  $A_1, A_2, A_T$  are  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ -modules, equations are of one of these types:

- Pairing product equations: with  $A_1 = \mathbb{G}_1, A_2 = \mathbb{G}_2, A_T = \mathbb{G}_T, f(x, y) = x \bullet y$ , the equation is  $(\mathcal{A} \cdot \mathcal{Y})(\mathcal{X} \cdot \mathcal{B})(\mathcal{X} \cdot \mathcal{F}\mathcal{Y}) = t_T$ .
- Multi-scalar multiplication in  $\mathbb{G}_1$ : with  $A_1 = \mathbb{G}_1, A_2 = \mathbb{Z}_p, A_T = \mathbb{G}_1, f(\mathcal{X}, y) = y\mathcal{X}$ , the equation is  $\mathcal{A} \cdot \mathbf{y} + \mathcal{X} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathcal{X} \cdot \Gamma \mathbf{y} = \mathcal{T}_1$ .
- Multi-scalar multiplication in  $\mathbb{G}_2$ : with  $A_1 = \mathbb{Z}_p, A_2 = \mathbb{G}_2, A_T = \mathbb{G}_2, f(x, \mathcal{Y}) = x\mathcal{Y}$ , the equation is  $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathcal{Y} + \mathbf{x} \cdot \mathcal{B} + \mathbf{x} \cdot \mathcal{Y} \Gamma = \mathcal{T}_2$ .
- Quadratic equation in  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ : with  $A_1 = \mathbb{Z}_p, A_2 = \mathbb{Z}_p, A_T = \mathbb{Z}_p$ ,  $f(x,y) = xy \mod p$ , the equation is  $\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{y} + \mathbf{x} \cdot \mathbf{b} + \mathbf{x} \cdot \Gamma \mathbf{y} = t$ .

In summary, the GS proof system allows constructing NIWI and NIZK proofs for satisfiability of a set of equations of the type above, which means that there is a choice of variables (witness) satisfying all equations simultaneously.

{36}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-36-0"></span>To construct the proof systems, Groth and Sahai gave a commitment to each element of the witness and "plug in" the commitments in place of the variables since the commitments themselves also "behave" like the values being committed to [GS08]. With some additional information, i.e., the proof and the properties of the commitment, they construct the proof system successfully. We then give the construction of the GS proof system used in this paper.

**Commitments.** The commitment scheme uses public parameters consisting of a matrix  $\mathbf{D} \leftarrow \mathcal{D}_k$  and vectors  $\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{t} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{k+1}$ . The commitment key is set as  $\mathbf{U} \coloneqq [\mathbf{D} \mid \mathbf{z}]$ . We consider two parameter regimes:

- **Perfectly Binding Mode:** For scalar vectors:  $\mathbf{z} \notin \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ . For group vectors:  $\mathbf{z} \in \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$  and  $\mathbf{t} \notin \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ .
- Perfectly Hiding Mode: For scalar vectors:  $\mathbf{z} \in \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ . For group vectors:  $\mathbf{z} \notin \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$  and  $\mathbf{t} \in \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ .

The two parameter regimes are computationally indistinguishable based on the  $\mathcal{D}_k\text{-MDDH}$  assumption.

**Scalar Commitments:** To commit to a scalar vector  $\mathbf{w} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  with randomness  $\mathbf{R} \leftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times n}\right)$ , we compute:

$$\begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{w}} \end{bmatrix} := \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{D} \end{bmatrix} \mathbf{R} + \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{z} \end{bmatrix} \mathbf{w}^\intercal$$

**Group Element Commitments:** To commit to a group vector  $[\mathbf{w}] \in \mathbb{G}^n$  with randomness  $\mathbf{R} \leftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_\mathsf{p}^{(k+1)\times n}\right)$ , we compute:

$$\left[\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{w}}\right] := \left[\mathbf{U}\right]\mathbf{R} + \mathbf{t}\left[\mathbf{w}^\intercal\right]$$

Dual-Mode Properties and Mathematical Foundation: The commitment scheme exhibits dual-mode behavior essential for zero-knowledge proofs and simulation soundness. In the perfectly binding mode, commitments uniquely determine the committed values, ensuring soundness by preventing adversaries from generating false proofs. In the perfectly hiding mode, commitments statistically hide the committed values, enabling zero-knowledge simulation where the simulator can create indistinguishable fake proofs without knowing the witness.

The mathematical foundation relies on the linear algebraic structure of the commitment scheme. For **scalar commitments**, in the binding mode when  $\mathbf{z} \notin \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ , the commitment matrix  $[\mathbf{D} \mid \mathbf{z}]$  has full rank, making the commitment function injective—each commitment value corresponds to a unique message. In the hiding mode when  $\mathbf{z} \in \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ , there exists a vector  $\mathbf{r}$  such that  $\mathbf{z} = \mathbf{Dr}$ , allowing the commitment  $[\mathbf{D}] \mathbf{R} + [\mathbf{z}] \mathbf{w}^{\intercal} = [\mathbf{D}] (\mathbf{R} + \mathbf{r} \mathbf{w}^{\intercal})$  to be perfectly randomized by choosing appropriate  $\mathbf{R}$ , thus statistically hiding the message  $\mathbf{w}$ .

For **group element commitments**, the dual-mode property operates through a complementary mechanism: in the binding mode, we have  $\mathbf{z} \in \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$  and  $\mathbf{t} \notin \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ , ensuring that the commitment  $[\mathbf{U}] \mathbf{R} + \mathbf{t} [\mathbf{w}^\intercal]$  uniquely determines  $[\mathbf{w}]$  since  $\mathbf{t}$  provides the necessary linear independence. In the hiding

{37}------------------------------------------------

mode,  $\mathbf{z} \notin \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$  and  $\mathbf{t} \in \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ , where  $\mathbf{t} = \mathbf{D}\mathbf{s}$  for some  $\mathbf{s}$ , allowing the commitment to be rewritten as  $[\mathbf{U}] \mathbf{R} + \mathbf{D}\mathbf{s} [\mathbf{w}^{\intercal}] = [\mathbf{U}] (\mathbf{R} + [\mathbf{I}_k \mid \mathbf{0}]\mathbf{s} [\mathbf{w}^{\intercal}])$ , which can be perfectly randomized.

The  $\mathcal{D}_k$ -MDDH assumption ensures that distinguishing between the parameter regimes (whether vectors are in the span or not) is computationally infeasible, enabling the seamless transition between binding and hiding properties and allowing the proof system to achieve both soundness and zero-knowledge simultaneously through a standard hybrid argument.

**Notation:** For the proof system, we use the commitment schemes defined above. To commit to scalar witnesses  $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^m$ , we compute  $\mathbf{c} = [\mathbf{D}] \mathbf{R} + [\mathbf{z}] \mathbf{x}^\intercal$  with randomness  $\mathbf{R} \leftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times m}\right)$ . To commit to group witnesses  $[\mathbf{y}] \in \mathbb{G}^n$ , we compute  $\mathbf{d} = [\mathbf{U}] \mathbf{S} + \mathbf{t} [\mathbf{y}^\intercal]$  with randomness  $\mathbf{S} \leftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_p^{(k+1) \times n}\right)$ .

We define different types of bilinear operations depending on the input types:

- Scalar-Commitment Operation: For scalar  $\mathbf{a} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  and commitment  $\mathbf{d} \in \mathbb{G}^{k+1}$ :

$$\mathbf{a} \bullet \mathbf{d} = \sum_{i=1}^{n} a_i \cdot d_i$$

where  $\cdot$  denotes scalar multiplication in the group.

- Commitment-Group Operation: For commitment  $\mathbf{c} \in \mathbb{G}^{k+1}$  and group element  $[\mathbf{b}] \in \mathbb{G}^m$ :

$$\mathbf{c} \bullet [\mathbf{b}] = \sum_{i=1}^{k+1} \sum_{j=1}^{m} c_i \bullet b_j$$

where • denotes the bilinear pairing.

- Commitment-Commitment Operation: For commitments  $\mathbf{c}, \mathbf{d} \in \mathbb{G}^{k+1}$ :

$$\mathbf{c} \bullet \mathbf{d} = \sum_{i=1}^{k+1} \sum_{j=1}^{k+1} c_i \bullet d_j$$

where • denotes the bilinear pairing.

**Proof** To prove the equation  $\mathbf{a} \cdot [\mathbf{y}] + \mathbf{x} \cdot [\mathbf{b}] + \mathbf{x} \cdot \Gamma [\mathbf{y}] = [t]$ , the prover proceeds as follows:

- 1. Commit to the witnesses: Compute commitments  $\mathbf{c} = [\mathbf{D}] \mathbf{R} + [\mathbf{z}] \mathbf{x}^{\mathsf{T}}$  and  $\mathbf{d} = [\mathbf{U}] \mathbf{S} + \mathbf{t} [\mathbf{y}^{\mathsf{T}}]$  using randomness  $\mathbf{R} \leftarrow \mathsf{U} (\mathbb{Z}_{\mathsf{p}}^{k \times m})$  and  $\mathbf{S} \leftarrow \mathsf{U} (\mathbb{Z}_{\mathsf{p}}^{(k+1) \times n})$ .
- 2. Generate proof elements: Sample  $\mathbf{T} \leftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_\mathsf{p}^{k \times (k+1)}\right)$  and compute:

$$\pi := \mathbf{R}^{\intercal} \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{b} \end{bmatrix} + \mathbf{R}^{\intercal} \Gamma(\begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{U} \end{bmatrix} \mathbf{S} + \mathbf{t} \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{y}^{\intercal} \end{bmatrix}) - \mathbf{T} \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{z} \end{bmatrix}$$
$$\theta := \mathbf{S}^{\intercal} \mathbf{a} + \mathbf{S}^{\intercal} (\Gamma^{\intercal} \mathbf{x}) + \mathbf{T}^{\intercal} \mathbf{t}$$

where the operations are performed component-wise for group elements.

3. Return the proof  $(\mathbf{c}, \mathbf{d}, \pi, \theta)$ .

{38}------------------------------------------------

**Verification** Given a proof  $(\mathbf{c}, \mathbf{d}, \pi, \theta)$  for the equation  $\mathbf{a} \cdot [\mathbf{y}] + \mathbf{x} \cdot [\mathbf{b}] + \mathbf{x} \cdot \Gamma[\mathbf{y}] = [t]$ , the verifier checks whether the following equation holds in the target group  $\mathbb{G}_{\mathsf{T}}$ :

$$\mathbf{a} \bullet \mathbf{d} + \mathbf{c} \bullet [\mathbf{b}] + \mathbf{c} \bullet (\Gamma \mathbf{d}) = e([1], [t]) + [\mathbf{z}] \bullet \pi + \theta \bullet \mathbf{t}$$

where e([1],[t]) represents the pairing of the generator with [t]. The verification accepts if and only if the above equation holds.

## C Security Game Descriptions for aHRA-secure PRE

This appendix provides detailed descriptions of the hybrid security games used in the proofs of Theorem 1 and Theorem 2. These games formalize the sequence of indistinguishable modifications applied to the security experiment to establish tight adaptive security for our proxy re-encryption schemes.

## D Language-Malleable Zero-Knowledge Proof with Special Simulation Soundness

We adapt the notion of simulation soundness from Sahai [Sah99] to the tagbased setting. Our definition differs from the existing notions of weak and strong unbounded simulation soundness from [AJO<sup>+</sup>19]. As noted in [AJO<sup>+</sup>19], weak unbounded simulation soundness is insufficient for building CCA-secure protocols. This is because it allows an adversary to generate a new proof for the same statement contained in a challenge ciphertext, and then submit this modified ciphertext to a decryption oracle.

Conversely, the stronger notion of unbounded simulation soundness is overly restrictive for our application. This notion forbids an adversary from creating a valid proof for any false statement, thereby precluding any form of proof malleability. Such a strict requirement is incompatible with proxy re-encryption, where the core functionality relies on the ability to transform a proof for an original ciphertext into a new, valid proof for a re-encrypted ciphertext.

To bridge this gap, we introduce the notion of *special simulation soundness*. This notion permits a controlled form of malleability, allowing an adversary to

{40}------------------------------------------------

$$\begin{array}{|c|c|c|} \hline \mathbf{Alg} \ \mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{Chall},b}(i,\mathsf{m}_0,\mathsf{m}_1) \colon \\ \hline 01 \ x \overset{\$}{\sim} \ \mathsf{Sample}_{\mathcal{X}}(1^{\lambda}) & & & & & & & & & & & & &$$

<span id="page-40-0"></span>**Fig. 9.** Hybrid games for aHRA security from  $G_5$  onwards. Let  $lpk_1$  define a linear language of dimension k over an ambient space of dimension  $\ell$ . The original language  $lpk_0$  has dimension k over an ambient space of dimension  $\ell$ .

transform a simulated proof for a given statement into a new, valid proof for a related, but otherwise false, statement. This controlled malleability is formalized through the concept of a trapdoor language distribution, which we define below.

#### Definition 12 (Membership-Preserving Language Family).

A membership-preserving language family MPL with respect to a map family  $\mathcal{H} = \{\mathsf{H}_i\} = \{(\mathsf{H}_i^{\mathsf{lpk}}, \mathsf{H}_i^{\mathsf{x}}, \mathsf{H}_i^{\mathsf{w}})\}$  consists of three PPT algorithms MPL = (Setup, Samp, Test) with the following syntax:

- $\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda) \to (\mathsf{lpk}, \mathsf{td})$ : Takes a security parameter  $\lambda$  as input, and outputs a language public key  $\mathsf{lpk}$  and  $trapdoor\ \mathsf{td}$ .
- $\mathsf{Samp}(\mathsf{lpk}) \to (\mathsf{x}, \mathsf{w}) \colon \mathit{Takes} \ \mathit{a} \ \mathit{public} \ \mathit{key} \ \mathsf{lpk} \ \mathit{as input}, \ \mathit{and outputs} \ \mathit{a} \ \mathit{pair} \ (\mathsf{x}, \mathsf{w}) \ \mathit{such that} \ \mathsf{x} \in \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}} \ \mathit{and} \ (\mathsf{x}, \mathsf{w}) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{lpk}}.$
- Test(lpk, x, w, td)  $\rightarrow$  {0,1}: Takes a public key lpk, a statement x, a witness w, and a trapdoor td as input, and outputs a bit indicating whether  $x \in \mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ .

We require the following properties.

Correctness. For any security parameter  $\lambda$ , we have

$$\Pr \bigg[ \mathsf{Test}(\mathsf{lpk},\mathsf{x},\mathsf{w},\mathsf{td}) = 1 \; \bigg| \; \frac{(\mathsf{lpk},\mathsf{td}) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})}{(\mathsf{x},\mathsf{w}) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \mathsf{Samp}(\mathsf{lpk})} \hspace{0.1em} \bigg] = 1.$$

**Membership-Preserving.** We say that MPL is membership-preserving with respect to a function family  $\mathcal{H} = \{\mathsf{H}_i\} = \{(\mathsf{H}_i^{\mathsf{lpk}}, \mathsf{H}_i^{\mathsf{x}}, \mathsf{H}_i^{\mathsf{w}})\}$ , if for any security parameter  $\lambda$ , any public key  $\mathsf{lpk}$ , any statement  $\mathsf{x}$ , let  $\mathsf{lpk}' := \mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{lpk}}(\mathsf{lpk})$ ,  $\mathsf{x}' = \mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{x}}(\mathsf{x})$  and  $\mathsf{w}' = \mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{w}}(\mathsf{w})$ , we have

$$x \in_{\mathsf{w}} \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}} \iff x' \in_{\mathsf{w}'} \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}'}.$$

{41}------------------------------------------------

To formalize the notion of special simulation soundness, we introduce a symmetric link function, link:  $(\mathcal{X}, \mathcal{L})^2 \to \{0,1\}$ , which specifies when two statement-language pairs are considered linked. This function is designed to capture the controlled malleability of the proof system. In the context of our proxy re-encryption scheme, for instance, two statements (i.e., ciphertexts) are linked if one can be transformed into the other via re-encryption. Consequently, the special simulation soundness property must permit this form of malleability. That is, the production of a new proof for a statement-language pair linked to a previously simulated one does not constitute a break of soundness. Taking a MPL as an example, we have

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{link}(\mathsf{x},\mathsf{lpk},\mathsf{x}',\mathsf{lpk}') &= 1 \implies (\mathsf{x}' = \mathsf{H}^\mathsf{x}(\mathsf{x}) \land \mathsf{lpk}' = \mathsf{H}^\mathsf{lpk}(\mathsf{lpk})) \\ & \lor (\mathsf{x} = \mathsf{H}^\mathsf{x}(\mathsf{x}') \land \mathsf{lpk} = \mathsf{H}^\mathsf{lpk}(\mathsf{lpk}')). \end{split}$$

**Definition 13 (Language Malleable Tag-based NIZK).** A language-malleable tag-based NIZK proof system for a membership-preserving language family MPL with respect to a function family  $\mathcal{H} = \{\mathsf{H}_i\} = \{(\mathsf{H}_i^{\mathsf{lpk}}, \mathsf{H}_i^{\mathsf{x}}, \mathsf{H}_i^{\mathsf{w}})\}^{20}$  consists of four PPT algorithms  $\Pi = (\mathsf{Setup}, \mathsf{Prove}, \mathsf{Ver}, \mathsf{Trans})$  with the following syntax:

- Setup $(1^{\lambda}) \to \text{crs}$ : Takes a security parameter  $\lambda$  as input, and outputs a common reference string crs.
- Prove(crs, lpk, x, w)  $\rightarrow$  (t,  $\pi$ ): Takes a crs, a language public key lpk, a statement x, and a witness w for the language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$  as input, and outputs a tag t and a proof  $\pi$ .
- Ver(crs, lpk, t, x,  $\pi$ )  $\rightarrow$  {0,1}: Takes a crs, a language public key lpk, a tag t, a statement x, and a proof  $\pi$  as input, and outputs a bit.
- Trans(crs, lpk, t, x,  $\pi$ , H<sup>lpk</sup>)  $\rightarrow$  (lpk', x',  $\pi$ '): Takes a crs, a language public key lpk, a tag t, a statement-proof pair (x,  $\pi$ ) for the language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ , and a key homomorphism H<sup>lpk</sup> as input, and outputs a language public key lpk' and a new statement-proof pair (x',  $\pi$ ') for the language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk'}$  := H<sup>lpk</sup>( $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ ) with the same tag t.

We require the following properties.

**Correctness.** For any security parameter  $\lambda$ , any  $i \in [N_U]$ , and any statement-witness pair  $(x, w) \in \mathcal{R}$ , we have:

$$\Pr\bigg[\mathsf{Ver}(\mathsf{crs},\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}},\mathsf{t},\mathsf{x},\pi) = 1 \; \bigg| \; \underset{(\mathsf{t},\pi)}{\mathsf{crs}} \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \\ (\mathsf{t},\pi) \overset{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \; \mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{crs},\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}},\mathsf{x},\mathsf{w}) \; \bigg] = 1.$$

**Zero-Knowledge.** There exists a PPT simulator S = (SSetup, Sim) such that for any PPT adversary A, the following advantage is negligible,

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ZK}}_{\varPi,\mathcal{A}}(1^\lambda) := & \left| \Pr \Big[ \mathcal{A}^{\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{Prove}}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{lpk},\cdot)}(\mathsf{crs}) = 1 \Big] \right. \\ & \left. - \Pr \Big[ \mathcal{A}^{\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{Sim}}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{lpk},\mathsf{simK},\cdot)}(\mathsf{crs}) = 1 \Big] \right|, \end{split}$$

<span id="page-41-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>20</sup> We assume that the identity function is included in  $\mathcal{H}$ .

{42}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-42-0"></span>where  $(\operatorname{crs}, \operatorname{sim} \mathsf{K}) \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \operatorname{\mathsf{SSetup}}(1^{\lambda})$  in the second probability, and  $\operatorname{\mathsf{crs}} \stackrel{\hspace{0.1em}\mathsf{\scriptscriptstyle\$}}{\leftarrow} \operatorname{\mathsf{Setup}}(1^{\lambda})$  in the first. The oracles only answer queries for statements  $\mathsf{x} \in \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}$ . Note that  $\operatorname{\mathsf{Prove}}_i$  and  $\operatorname{\mathsf{Sim}}_i$  can detect invalid statements using  $\operatorname{\mathsf{td}}$  for the trapdoor language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}}$ .

**Language Malleability.** We require that the proof transformation algorithm can efficiently transform a valid proof  $\pi$  for statement x in  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk}$  into a valid proof  $\pi'$  for a new statement x' in  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk'}$  with  $lpk' := H^{lpk}(lpk)$  with  $(H^{lpk}, \cdot, \cdot) \in \mathcal{H}$ . Formally, for any  $(x, w) \in \mathcal{L}_{lpk}$ , for any  $H^{lpk}$  such that  $(H^{lpk}, \cdot, \cdot) \in \mathcal{H}$ , we have.

$$\Pr\left[ \begin{array}{c} \mathsf{Ver}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{lpk}',\mathsf{t},\mathsf{x}',\pi') = 1 \left| \begin{matrix} \mathsf{crs} \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda) \\ (\mathsf{t},\pi) \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{lpk},\mathsf{x},\mathsf{w}) \\ \mathsf{lpk}' := \mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{lpk}}(\mathsf{lpk}) \\ (\mathsf{x}',\pi') \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{Trans}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{lpk},\mathsf{t},\mathsf{x},\pi,\mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{lpk}}) \end{matrix} \right] = 1.$$

**Special Simulation Soundness.** For any PPT adversary A, there exists a PPT simulator S = (SSetup, Sim) such that the following probability is negligible.

$$\Pr \left[ \begin{array}{c} \mathsf{Ver}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{lpk}^\star,\mathsf{t}^\star,\mathsf{x}^\star,\pi^\star) = 1 \\ \wedge \mathsf{x}^\star \notin \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{lpk}^\star} \\ \wedge \nexists (\mathsf{x},\mathsf{lpk}) \in \mathcal{T} : \mathsf{link}(\mathsf{x},\mathsf{lpk},\mathsf{x}^\star,\mathsf{lpk}^\star) = 1 \end{array} \right| \begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{simK}) \xleftarrow{\$} \mathsf{SSetup}(1^\lambda) \\ (\mathsf{lpk}^\star,\mathsf{x}^\star,\mathsf{t}^\star,\pi^\star) \\ \xleftarrow{\$} \mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{Sim}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{simK},\cdot)}(\mathsf{crs}) \end{array} \right],$$

where  $\mathcal{T}$  is the set of tags and language public keys generated via the simulation oracle queries  $\mathcal{A}$ , and link $(x_1, lpk_1, x_2, lpk_2)$  is the predicate defining whether two statements are linked via the function family  $\mathcal{H}$ .

Our notion of special simulation soundness permits a controlled form of malleability. Specifically, the proof transformation function, Trans, allows for the derivation of a new, valid proof for a statement that is legitimately related to the original one (e.g., a re-encrypted ciphertext in a PRE scheme), even when starting from a simulated proof. This property positions our notion between existing definitions: it is stronger than weak simulation soundness, which allows arbitrary proof modifications for a given tag, but weaker than strong simulation soundness, which prohibits any malleation of proofs for false statements, regardless of the tag.

Relation to other simulation soundness notions The literature presents two distinct definitions of simulation soundness for tag-based NIZK proof systems. The weak simulation soundness defined in [AJO<sup>+</sup>19] does not prevent the adversary from generating a new proof for false statements with the same tag used in the simulated proof but prohibits generating a valid proof for any false statement with other tags. Conversely, the strong simulation soundness in [AJO<sup>+</sup>19] prevents the adversary from generating any new valid proof for false statements. Our notion of special simulation soundness lies between these two definitions. It is stronger than weak simulation soundness because the adversary cannot generate a valid proof for any false statement with tags different from the one in the simulated

{43}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-43-0"></span>proof. However, it is weaker than strong simulation soundness, as it allows the adversary to generate a new valid proof for some false statements with the same tag as the simulated proof. This relaxation is essential in the proxy re-encryption scheme, where the adversary can generate a new valid proof for re-encrypted ciphertexts with known tokens.

## D.1 Generic Construction of Special Simulation Sound tLM-NIZK

We give here the language family that we will use in the aCCA proxy re-encryption scheme. In our case, a language public key consists of a matrix  $[\mathbf{A}_i]_1$ , we need to prove the linear span language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}$  defined as follows:

$$\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i} := \{ \mathsf{x} = \left[\mathbf{u}\right]_1 \in \mathbb{G}_1^{\ell+1} \mid \exists \mathbf{w} \in \mathbb{Z}_\mathsf{p}^k : \mathsf{x} = \left[\mathbf{A}_i\right]_1 \mathbf{w} \}.$$

We define an extended language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}^{\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{ck}}$ , we will show that a proof of  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}^{\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{ck}}$  can be seen as a special simulation-sound tLM-NIZK proof of the language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}$ . Moreover, if the proof of  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}^{\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{ck}}$  has language malleability over the choice of  $\mathbf{A}_i$ , then the corresponding tLM-NIZK proof of  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}$  also has language malleability over the choice of  $\mathbf{A}_i$ . The extended language is defined as follows:

$$\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}^{(\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{ck})} := \left\{ (\mathsf{x},\mathbf{C_s}) \middle| \begin{array}{l} \exists \mathsf{w} \in \mathbb{Z}_\mathsf{p}^k : \mathsf{x} = \left[\mathbf{A}_i\right]_1 \mathsf{w} \\ \forall \exists \mathbf{A}_j, \mathsf{x}', \sigma, \mathsf{open} : \begin{cases} \mathbf{C_s} = \mathsf{Com.Commit}(\mathsf{ck}, \sigma; \mathsf{open}) \\ \land \mathsf{Ver}(\mathsf{svk}, \mathsf{H}(\mathbf{A}_j, \mathsf{x}', \mathsf{t}), \sigma) = 1 \\ \land \mathsf{link}(\mathsf{x}, \mathbf{A}_i, \mathsf{x}', \mathbf{A}_j) = 1 \\ \end{cases} \right\},$$

where (svk, ssk)  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  SIG.KGen(1 $^{\lambda}$ ) and ck  $\stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow}$  COM.KGen(1 $^{\lambda}$ ).

Remark 2. In our instantiation using the Groth-Sahai proof system [GS08], the use of commitment  $\mathbf{C_s}$  in the extended language definition is equivalent to the extractability property of GS08. Specifically, if an adversary can produce a valid proof for  $(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{C_s}) \in \mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}^{(\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{ck})}$  where  $\mathbf{x} \notin \mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}$ , then by the extractability of GS08, one can extract witnesses  $\sigma$ , open such that  $\mathbf{C_s} = \mathsf{Com}.\mathsf{Commit}(\mathsf{ck}, \sigma; \mathsf{open})$  and  $\mathsf{Ver}(\mathsf{svk}, \mathsf{H}(\mathbf{A}_j, \mathsf{x}', \mathsf{t}), \sigma) = 1$  for some  $\mathbf{A}_j, \mathsf{x}'$  with  $\mathsf{link}(\mathsf{x}, \mathbf{A}_i, \mathsf{x}', \mathbf{A}_j) = 1$ . The commitment mechanism thus transforms the existential quantification over signatures into a concrete extractable witness, which is essential for the security reduction to work properly in the GS framework.

**Theorem 4.** Let SIG = (KGen, Sig, Ver) be a signature scheme. Consider a language-malleable tLM-NIZK proof system  $\Pi_{SIG}$  for the extended language family  $\{\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}^{\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{ck}}\}_i$  with respect to the function family  $\mathcal{H}$ . If  $\Pi_{\mathsf{SIG}}$  is computationally sound, it can be interpreted as a special simulation-sound tLM-NIZK proof system, denoted  $\Pi$ , for the linear language family  $\{\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}\}_i$ . More formally, for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exist PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{B}_1, \mathcal{B}_2, \mathcal{B}_3$  such that:

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ZK}}_{\mathbf{\Pi},\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda}) &= \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ZK}}_{\mathbf{\Pi}_{\mathsf{SIG}},\mathcal{B}_1}(1^{\lambda}) + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Hiding}}_{\mathsf{COM},\mathcal{B}_4}(1^{\lambda}), \\ \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{SSS}}_{\mathbf{\Pi},\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda}) &\leq \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Sound}}_{\mathbf{\Pi}_{\mathsf{SIG}},\mathcal{B}_2}(1^{\lambda}) + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{UF-CMA}}_{\mathsf{SIG},\mathcal{B}_3}(1^{\lambda}) + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{Binding}}_{\mathsf{COM},\mathcal{B}_5}(1^{\lambda}). \end{split}$$

{44}------------------------------------------------

Consequently, if  $\Pi_{\mathsf{SIG}}$  is computationally sound and  $\mathsf{SIG}$  is UF-CMA (resp., one-time UF-CMA), then  $\Pi$  is special simulation-sound (resp., one-time special simulation-sound).

*Proof.* The correctness and language malleability of  $\Pi$  are direct consequences of the corresponding properties of  $\Pi_{SIG}$ , since a proof for the language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}$  is a specific instance of a proof for the extended language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}^{\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{ck}}$ . Specifically, an honest proof for  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}$  satisfies the first clause in the OR condition defining  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}^{\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{ck}}$ . Therefore, we omit the details for the correctness and language malleability.

We give more details for the zero-knowledge and special simulation-soundness property.

Zero-Knowledge. We construct a simulator for  $\Pi$ . The simulation key for  $\Pi$  is the signing key ssk of the signature scheme SIG. To simulate a proof for a statement  $x \in \mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}$  with tag t, the simulator first computes a signature  $\sigma$  on the hash value  $hv := H(\mathbf{A}_i, x, t)$  using its knowledge of ssk. It then commits to  $\sigma$  to obtain  $\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{s}}$ . Since the identity function is in  $\mathcal{H}$ , any statement is linked to itself, i.e.,  $link(\mathbf{A}_i, x, \mathbf{A}_i, x) = 1$ . Thus, the simulator can use the signature  $\sigma$  as a witness for the second clause of the extended language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}^{\mathsf{svk,ck}}$ . It then invokes the honest prover of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{SIG}}$  to generate a proof for the extended statement  $(x, \mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{s}}, \bot)$ . The indistinguishability of the simulated proof from a real one follows from the zero-knowledge property of  $\Pi_{\mathsf{SIG}}$  and the hiding property of the commitment scheme COM.

Special Simulation Soundness. The special simulation soundness of  $\Pi$  relies on the computational soundness of  $\Pi_{SIG}$ , the unforgeability of the signature scheme SIG, and the binding property of the commitment scheme COM. Suppose an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  breaks the special simulation soundness of  $\Pi$ . It outputs a valid proof  $(t^*, \pi^*)$  for a false statement  $x^* \notin \mathcal{L}_{lpk^*}$ , where the pair  $(lpk^*, x^*)$  is not linked to any pair (lpk, x) from the simulation oracle queries. Since  $x^*$  is a false statement, the witness for the proof  $\pi^*$  must satisfy the second clause of the extended language  $\mathcal{L}_{lpk^*}^{svk,ck}$ . This implies that the proof contains a commitment  $\mathbf{C_s}$  to a valid signature  $\sigma$  on  $hv^* = H(\mathbf{A}_j, x', t^*)$  for some linked pair  $(\mathbf{A}_j, x')$ . Because  $(lpk^*, x^*)$  is not linked to any prior query, the hash value  $hv^*$  has not been signed by the simulation oracle. This constitutes a forgery against the signature scheme SIG. A formal reduction can be constructed where an adversary breaking the special simulation soundness of  $\Pi$  is used to break either the computational soundness of  $\Pi_{SIG}$ , the unforgeability of SIG, or the binding property of COM.

Language Family Specification To construct our aCCA-secure proxy reencryption schemes, we first specify the language family  $\{\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}\}_i$  and the corresponding transformation functions  $\mathcal{H}$ . Let  $\mathsf{N}_\mathsf{U}$  be a positive integer. The construction involves a set of matrices  $\{\mathbf{A}_i\}_{i\in[\mathsf{N}_\mathsf{U}]}$ , where each  $\mathbf{A}_i\in\mathbb{Z}_\mathsf{p}^{(\ell+1)\times k}$ . Each matrix  $\mathbf{A}_i$  is partitioned into its top  $\ell$  rows, denoted by  $\bar{\mathbf{A}}_i\in\mathbb{Z}_\mathsf{p}^{\ell\times k}$ , and its bottom row, denoted by  $\bar{\mathbf{A}}_i\in\mathbb{Z}_\mathsf{p}^{1\times k}$ .

{45}------------------------------------------------

All matrices  $\mathbf{A}_i$  for  $i \in [\mathsf{N}_\mathsf{U}]$  share a common sub-matrix  $\bar{\mathbf{A}} \in \mathbb{Z}_\mathsf{p}^{\ell \times k}$  comprising their top  $\ell$  rows. The bottom row  $\underline{\mathbf{A}}_i$  of each matrix  $\mathbf{A}_i$  is defined based on a common vector  $\mathbf{x} \leftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_\mathsf{p}^k\right)$  and a set of unique vectors  $\{\bar{\mathbf{k}}_i \leftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_\mathsf{p}^\ell\right)\}_{i \in [\mathsf{N}_\mathsf{U}]}$ . Specifically, for each  $i \in [\mathsf{N}_\mathsf{U}]$ , the bottom row is computed as  $\underline{\mathbf{A}}_i := \mathbf{x}^\intercal + \bar{\mathbf{k}}_i^\intercal \bar{\mathbf{A}}$ .

With these definitions, we can specify the language family and the transformation functions. For each  $i \in [N_U]$ , we define a vector  $\mathbf{k}_i := (\bar{\mathbf{k}}_i^{\mathsf{T}}, 1)^{\mathsf{T}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell+1}$ . The transformation from index i to j is determined by the difference vector  $(d_1, \ldots, d_\ell, 0)^{\mathsf{T}} := \mathbf{k}_j - \mathbf{k}_i$ . This vector is used to construct a transformation matrix  $\Delta_{i,j} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(\ell+1)\times(\ell+1)}$  as follows:

$$\bm{\Delta}_{i,j} := \begin{pmatrix} 1 & 0 & \dots & 0 & 0 \ 0 & 1 & \dots & 0 & 0 \ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots & \vdots \ 0 & 0 & \dots & 1 & 0 \ d_1 & d_2 & \dots & d_\ell & 1 \end{pmatrix}.$$

By construction, this matrix maps  $\mathbf{A}_i$  to  $\mathbf{A}_j$ , i.e.,  $\mathbf{A}_j = \boldsymbol{\Delta}_{i,j} \mathbf{A}_i$ . The language family and transformation functions are then defined as follows:

– The language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}$  is the linear span language defined by

$$\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i} := \{ \mathsf{x} \in \mathbb{G}_1^{\ell+1} \mid \exists \mathbf{r} \in \mathbb{Z}_\mathsf{p}^k : \mathsf{x} = \left[ \mathbf{A}_i \right]_1 \mathbf{r} \}.$$

- The transformation function family  $\mathcal{H} = \{(\mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{lpk}}_{i,j},\mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{x}}_{i,j},\mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{w}}_{i,j})\}_{i,j\in[\mathsf{N}_{\mathsf{U}}]}$  consists of:
  - A language public key transformation  $\mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{lpk}}_{i,j}: \mathbb{Z}^{(\ell+1)\times k}_{\mathsf{p}} \to \mathbb{Z}^{(\ell+1)\times k}_{\mathsf{p}}$  that maps  $\mathbf{A}_i$  to  $\mathbf{A}_j$  via left-multiplication by  $\mathbf{\Delta}_{i,j}$ :

$$\mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{lpk}}_{i,j}(\mathbf{A}_i) := \mathbf{\Delta}_{i,j}\mathbf{A}_i = \mathbf{A}_j.$$

• A statement transformation  $\mathsf{H}_{i,j}^{\mathsf{x}}:\mathbb{G}_1^{\ell+1}\to\mathbb{G}_1^{\ell+1}$  that maps a statement  $\mathsf{x}_i\in\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}$  to a statement  $\mathsf{x}_j\in\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_j}$  via left-multiplication by  $\boldsymbol{\Delta}_{i,j}$ :

$$\mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{x}}_{i,j}(\mathsf{x}_i) := \mathbf{\Delta}_{i,j} \mathsf{x}_i = \mathsf{x}_j.$$

• A witness transformation  $\mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{w}}_{i,j}: \mathbb{Z}^k_{\mathsf{p}} \to \mathbb{Z}^k_{\mathsf{p}}$  that maps a witness  $\mathsf{w}_i$  to a witness  $\mathsf{w}_j$  via identity function:

$$\mathsf{H}^{\mathsf{w}}_{i,j}(\mathsf{w}_i) := \mathsf{w}_i = \mathsf{w}_j.$$

We provide the full instantiation of our one-time and unbounded special simulation-sound tLM-NIZK proof system in Section E. The construction is based on a Groth-Sahai proof for the language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}^{(\mathsf{svk},\mathsf{ck})}$ . The language malleability property is a direct consequence of the linearity inherent in the Groth-Sahai framework.

{46}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-46-0"></span>E Instantiation of Special Simulation Sound tLM-NIZK

To instantiate the generic construction from D.1, we first fix the following public parameters and commitment algorithms.

To commit a vector  $\mathbf{w} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  or  $[\mathbf{w}] \in \mathbb{G}^n$ , we proceed as follows:

Public parameters: Sample  $\mathbf{D} \leftarrow \mathcal{D}_k$ , choose  $\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{y} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{k+1}$ , and set  $\mathbf{U} \coloneqq [\mathbf{D} | \mathbf{z}]$ .

We consider two parameter regimes:

- Perfectly Binding Mode:

Scalar vectors:  $\mathbf{z} \notin \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ .

Group vectors:  $\mathbf{z} \in \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$  and  $\mathbf{y} \notin \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ .

- Perfectly Hiding Mode:

Scalar vectors:  $z \in Span(D)$ .

Group vectors:  $\mathbf{z} \notin \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$  and  $\mathbf{y} \in \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ .

**Commitments:** Given the parameters above, commitments are computed as follows:

- Scalar  $\mathbf{w} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$ :  $\left[\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{w}}\right] := \left[\mathbf{D}\right] \mathbf{R} + \left[\mathbf{z}\right] \mathbf{w}^{\intercal}$ , where we sample  $\mathbf{R} \leftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_p^{k \times n}\right)$ .
- Group vector  $[\mathbf{w}] \in \mathbb{G}^n$ :  $[\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{w}}] := [\mathbf{U}] \mathbf{R} + \mathbf{y} [\mathbf{w}^{\intercal}]$ , where we sample  $\mathbf{R} \leftarrow \mathsf{U} \left( \mathbb{Z}_{\mathsf{p}}^{(k+1) \times n} \right)$ .

We use subscripts to indicate whether the public parameters are instantiated in  $\mathbb{G}_1$  or  $\mathbb{G}_2$ .

### E.1 One-Time Special Simulation Sound tLM-NIZK

To instantiate the one-time special simulation sound tLM-NIZK with tight security, we proceed by the following two steps

- We use the tightly-secure one-time structure preserving signature OTS given in [KPW15] to instantiate the underlying signature scheme.
- We use the Groth-Sahai proof system [GS08] to instantiate the language-malleable tLM-NIZK proof system  $\Pi_{SIG}$  for the extended language family  $\{\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}^{\mathsf{svk}}\}_i$  with respect to the function family  $\mathcal{H}$ .

We first recall the signature  $\mathsf{OTS} = (\mathsf{KGen}, \mathsf{Sig}, \mathsf{Ver})$  in Figure 10. To be compatible with the other part of the  $\mathsf{tLM}\text{-NIZK}$ , we exchange  $\mathbb{G}_1$  and  $\mathbb{G}_2$  in the original scheme. Due to the symmetric nature of pairing computation, this modification does not affect the correctness and security of the scheme.

**Theorem 5** ([KPW15]). The signature scheme OTS in Figure 10 is one-time UF-CMA. Concretely, for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists an PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  with  $\mathbf{T}(\mathcal{A}) \approx \mathbf{T}(\mathcal{B})$  and

$$\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{OTS},\mathcal{A}}^{\mathsf{OT\text{-}CMA}}(\lambda) \leq \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{D}_k,\mathsf{GGen},\mathcal{B}}^{\mathsf{KerMDH}}(\lambda) + 1/q.$$

We translate the statements of  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{A}_i}^{\mathsf{svk}}$  as follows:

{47}------------------------------------------------

$$\begin{array}{|c|c|c|} \hline \textbf{Alg KGen}(1^{\lambda}): \\ \hline 01 & \textbf{B} \hookleftarrow \mathcal{D}_{k} \\ \hline 02 & \textbf{K} \hookleftarrow \textbf{U} \left( \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{(n+1)\times(k+1)} \right) \\ \hline 03 & \textbf{C} = \textbf{KB} \\ \hline 04 & \text{svk} := \left( \begin{bmatrix} \textbf{B}^{\intercal} \end{bmatrix}_{1}, \begin{bmatrix} \textbf{C}^{\intercal} \end{bmatrix}_{1} \right) \\ \hline 05 & \text{ssk} := \textbf{K} \\ \hline 06 & \textbf{return} & (\text{svk}, \text{ssk}) \\ \hline \end{array} \begin{array}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c$$

where  $hv = H(\underline{\mathbf{A}}^*, \underline{\mathbf{x}}^*, \mathbf{t})$ .

Remark 3. In our proxy re-encryption setting, a ciphertext consists of three components  $(\mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{ct}_1, \mathsf{ct}_2)$ , where  $(\mathsf{ct}_0, \mathsf{ct}_1)$  together form the statement x used in this proof system, with  $\mathsf{ct}_1$  corresponding to the bottom row  $\underline{\mathsf{x}}$ . A complete link verification should ensure: (1) the transformational relationship of  $\mathsf{ct}_1$  (the bottom component), and (2) the invariance of  $\mathsf{ct}_0$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_2$  between linked ciphertexts.

The hash function and link verification equations above only address the bottom row relationships, corresponding to the  $\mathsf{ct}_1$  component. However, in our CCA construction, we employ an external signature scheme to authenticate  $\mathsf{ct}_0$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_2$ , ensuring their invariance across linked ciphertexts. Since the hash value incorporates the tag t, any signature reuse necessarily implies tag reuse, which would be detected by the signature verification in the outer CCA protocol. Therefore, this tLM-NIZK system focuses solely on proving the correct transformation of  $\mathsf{ct}_1$ , while the complete link property is jointly guaranteed by this proof system and the external signature scheme.

Moreover, we prove the first equation using the vector  $\mathbf{z_A}$  to commit elements in  $\mathbb{G}_2$ , and the last three equations are proved using  $\mathbf{z}_{\sigma}$  to commit elements in  $\mathbb{G}_2$ . Moreover, we have  $\mathbf{z} = \mathbf{z_A} + \mathbf{z_{\sigma}} \notin \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ , which ensures either the first two equations or the last three equations are proved in the soundness setting. Noticing that all commitments in  $\mathbb{G}_1$  are in the perfectly binding mode. We give all the commitments as follows

$$- \ \left[ \mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{w}} \right]_2 := \left[ \mathbf{D}_2 \right]_2 \mathbf{R}_{\mathbf{w}} + \left[ \mathbf{z}_{\mathbf{A}} \right]_2 \mathbf{w}^\intercal$$

<span id="page-47-2"></span>Note that x inherently consists of group elements in  $\mathbb{G}_1$ , but we explicitly use the notation  $[\cdot]_1$  here to emphasize its group properties for clarity. We adopt this consistent notation throughout the subsequent presentation.

{48}------------------------------------------------

$$\begin{array}{l} - \left[ \mathbf{C_k} \right]_2 := \left[ \mathbf{D_2} \right]_2 \mathbf{R_k} + \left[ \mathbf{z_\sigma} \right]_2 (\bar{\mathbf{k}}^\star)^\intercal \\ - \left[ \mathbf{C_\sigma} \right]_2 := \left[ \sigma \right]_2 \mathbf{R_s} + \left[ \mathbf{z_\sigma} \right]_2 \sigma^\intercal \end{array}$$

The verification equations are given as follows:

$$\begin{split} &-\left[\mathbf{A}_{i}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{w}}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}-\left[\mathbf{x}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{z}_{\mathbf{A}}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}=\left[\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{A}}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{D}_{2}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}\\ &-\left[\underline{\mathbf{A}}_{i}^{\intercal}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{z}_{\sigma}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}-\left[\left(\underline{\mathbf{A}}^{\star}\right)^{\intercal}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{z}_{\sigma}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}-\left[\overline{\mathbf{A}}^{\intercal}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{k}}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}=\left[\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{d}}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{D}_{2}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}\\ &-\left[\underline{\mathbf{x}}^{\intercal}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{z}_{\sigma}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}-\left[\left(\underline{\mathbf{x}}^{\star}\right)^{\intercal}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{z}_{\sigma}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}-\left[\overline{\mathbf{x}}^{\intercal}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{k}}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}=\left[\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{x}}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{D}_{2}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}\\ &-\left[\mathbf{B}^{\intercal}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{C}_{\sigma}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}-\left[\mathbf{C}^{\intercal}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\begin{pmatrix}1\\\mathsf{hv}\end{pmatrix}\mathbf{z}_{\sigma}^{\intercal}\right]_{2}=\left[\mathbf{P}_{\sigma}\right]_{1}\bullet\left[\mathbf{D}_{2}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} \end{split}$$

We give the full construction of the one-time special simulation sound tLM-NIZK proof system in Figure 11. We show in Figure 12 that the construction is malleable with respect to the transformation set  $\mathcal{H}$ .

```
\frac{\mathbf{Alg}\ \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})}{\mathtt{O1}\ \mathbf{D} \hookleftarrow \mathcal{D}_k}
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                             \frac{\mathbf{Alg\ Prove}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{t},\mathsf{x},\mathbf{w}):}{14\ \left[\underline{\mathbf{A}}^{\star}\right]_{1} \hookleftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{G}_{1}^{1\times k}\right)}
```

<span id="page-49-3"></span><span id="page-49-2"></span>**Theorem 6 (Zero-Knowledge).** The one-time tLM-NIZK proof system in Figure 11 is zero-knowledge. Concretely, for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists an PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  such that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ZK}}_{\Pi_{\mathsf{OT}},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq 2\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{KerMDH}}_{\mathcal{D}_k,\mathsf{GGen},\mathcal{B}}(\lambda).$$

{50}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-50-0"></span>Theorem 7 (Special Simulation Sound). The one-time tLM-NIZK proof system presented in Figure 11 satisfies special simulation soundness.

#### E.2 Special Simulation Sound tLM-NIZK

This section details the construction of an unbounded special simulation sound tLM-NIZK scheme  $\Pi_U$ , employing a framework similar to that used for the one-time special simulation sound tLM-NIZK construction in Figure 13. We replace the one-time signature scheme with a tightly-secure UF-CMA signature scheme and change the number of rows in matrix  $\bar{\bf A}$  from k+1 to  $\ell$ . The detailed construction of the proof system  $\Pi_U$  is presented in Figure 16.

We first recall the almost tightly-secure UF-CMA signature scheme [GHKP18] in Figure 14. The signature scheme is almost tightly-secure under the  $\mathcal{D}_k$ -MDDH assumption. We modify the signature scheme to sign a message  $\mathbf{m} \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  and the signature is of the form  $\sigma = ([\mathbf{t}]_1, \pi, [\mathbf{s}]_1)$ .

```
 \begin{array}{lll} \hline \textbf{Alg KGen}(1^{\lambda}): \\ \hline 01 \ \text{crs} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\mathbf{B}_0,\mathbf{B}_1}^{\vee}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \\ \hline 02 \ \mathbf{B}_0, \mathbf{B}_1 \hookleftarrow \mathcal{D}_{2k,k} \\ \hline 03 \ \mathbf{B} \hookleftarrow \mathcal{D}_k \\ \hline 04 \ \mathbf{K}_0, \mathbf{K}_1 \hookleftarrow \mathsf{U} \left(\mathbb{Z}_p^{2k \times (k+1)}\right) \\ \hline 05 \ \text{svk} := (\mathsf{crs}, \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{B}_0 \end{bmatrix}_1, \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{B} \end{bmatrix}_2, \\ \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{K}_0 \mathbf{B} \end{bmatrix}_2, \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{K}_1 \mathbf{B} \end{bmatrix}_2 \\ \hline \\ 06 \ \text{ssk} := (\mathbf{K}_0, \mathbf{K}_1) \\ \hline \\ 07 \ \mathbf{return} \ (\mathsf{svk}, \mathsf{ssk}) \\ \hline \end{array} \quad \begin{array}{l} \mathbf{Alg Sig}(\mathsf{ssk}, \mathsf{m} \in \mathbb{Z}_p): \\ \hline 08 \ \mathbf{r} \hookleftarrow \mathsf{U} \left(\mathbb{Z}_p^k\right); \ \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{t} \end{bmatrix}_1 := \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{B}_0 \end{bmatrix}_1 \mathbf{r} \\ \hline \\ 08 \ \mathbf{r} \hookleftarrow \mathsf{U} \left(\mathbb{Z}_p^k\right); \ \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{t} \end{bmatrix}_1 := \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{B}_0 \end{bmatrix}_1 \mathbf{r} \\ \hline \\ 09 \ \pi \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \Pi_{\mathbf{B}_0,\mathbf{B}_1}^{\mathsf{N}}.\mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{crs}, \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{t} \end{bmatrix}_1, \mathbf{r}) \\ \hline \\ 10 \ \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{s} \end{bmatrix}_1 := (\mathbf{K}_0 + \mathsf{m} \mathbf{K}_1)^\mathsf{T} \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{t} \end{bmatrix}_1 \\ \hline \\ 11 \ \mathbf{return} \ \sigma := (\begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{t} \end{bmatrix}_1, \pi, \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{s} \end{bmatrix}_1) \\ \hline \\ 12 \ \mathbf{check} \ \Pi_{\mathbf{B}_0,\mathbf{B}_1}^{\mathsf{N}}.\mathsf{Ver}(\mathsf{crs}, \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{t} \end{bmatrix}_1, \pi) \\ \hline \\ 13 \ \mathbf{check} \ \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{s} \end{bmatrix}_1 \neq \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{0} \end{bmatrix}_1 \\ \hline \\ 14 \ \mathbf{check} \ \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{s}^\mathsf{T} \end{bmatrix}_1 \bullet \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{K}_0 + \mathsf{m} \mathbf{K}_1 \end{bmatrix} \mathbf{B} \end{bmatrix}_2 \\ = \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{t}^\mathsf{T} \end{bmatrix}_1 \bullet \begin{bmatrix} (\mathbf{K}_0 + \mathsf{m} \mathbf{K}_1) \mathbf{B} \end{bmatrix}_2 \\ \hline \\ 15 \ \mathbf{return} \ 1 \\ \hline \end{array}
```

<span id="page-50-1"></span>Fig. 14. Construction of tightly-secure structure preserving signature scheme. [GHKP18]

For the sake of completeness, we also add the description of the OR proof system  $\Pi_{\mathbf{B}_0,\mathbf{B}_1}^{\vee}$  in Figure 15. We define the language as

$$\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{B}_{0},\mathbf{B}_{1}}^{\vee}=\left\{ \left[\mathbf{t}\right]_{2}\mid\exists\mathbf{r}\in\mathbb{Z}_{p}^{2k}.\left[\mathbf{t}\right]_{2}=\left[\mathbf{B}_{0}\right]_{2}\mathbf{r}\vee\left[\mathbf{t}\right]_{2}=\left[\mathbf{B}_{1}\right]_{2}\mathbf{r}\right\} .$$

Theorem 8 ([GHKP18, Theorem 2]). Let  $\Pi_{\mathbf{B}_0,\mathbf{B}_1}^{\vee}$  be a non-interactive zero-knowledge proof. The signature scheme in Figure 14 is tightly-secure under the  $\mathcal{D}_k$ -MDDH and the  $\mathcal{D}_{2k,k}$ -MDDH assumption.

Concretely, for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{B}_1, \dots, \mathcal{B}_4$  with  $\mathbf{T}(\mathcal{B}) \approx \mathbf{T}(\mathcal{A}) + Q \cdot \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  such that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{uf\text{-}cma}}_{\mathsf{Sig},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{MDDH}}_{\mathcal{D}_k,\mathbb{G}_2,\mathcal{B}_1}(\lambda) + \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{core}}_{\mathcal{B}_2}(\lambda) + \frac{Q}{p}.$$

{51}------------------------------------------------

```
Alg SSetup(1^{\lambda}):
    Alg Setup(1^{\lambda}):
  \overline{01 \mathbf{D} \hookleftarrow \mathcal{D}_k}
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 16 \mathbf{D} \hookleftarrow \mathcal{D}_k, \mathbf{u} \hookleftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^k
  02 \mathbf{z} \leftarrow \mathsf{U}\left(\mathbb{Z}_\mathsf{p}^{k+1} \setminus \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})\right)
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  17 \mathbf{z} := \mathbf{D} \cdot \mathbf{u}
  03 crs := ([\mathbf{D}]_2, [\mathbf{z}]_2)
04 return crs
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  18 \operatorname{crs} := (\mathbf{p}, \left[\mathbf{D}\right]_2, \left[\mathbf{z}\right]_2); \ \operatorname{td} := \mathbf{u}
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  19 return (crs, td)
  Alg Prove(crs, [\mathbf{t}]_1, \mathbf{r}):
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                \frac{\mathbf{Alg\ PVer(crs, \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{t} \end{bmatrix}}_1, \pi):}{20\ \left[ \mathbf{z}_1 \right]_2 := \left[ \mathbf{z} \right]_2 - \left[ \mathbf{z}_0 \right]_2}

\overline{05 \text{ let } j \in \{0,1\} \text{ s.t.}} \left[ \mathbf{t} \right]_1 = \left[ \mathbf{B}_j \right]_1 \cdot \mathbf{r}

  06 \mathbf{v} \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_{\mathsf{p}}^k
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                21 check \left[\mathbf{B}_{0}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{C}_{0}\right]_{2}

\begin{array}{ll}
07 & \left[\mathbf{z}_{1-j}\right]_{2} := \left[\mathbf{D}\right]_{2} \cdot \mathbf{v} \\
08 & \left[\mathbf{z}_{j}\right]_{2} := \left[\mathbf{z}\right]_{2} - \left[\mathbf{z}_{1-j}\right]_{2} \\
09 & \mathbf{S}_{0}, \mathbf{S}_{1} \leftrightarrow \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{k \times k} \\
10 & \left[\mathbf{C}_{j}\right]_{2} := \mathbf{S}_{j} \cdot \left[\mathbf{D}\right]_{2}^{\mathsf{T}} + \mathbf{r} \cdot \left[\mathbf{z}_{j}\right]_{2}^{\mathsf{T}}
\end{array}

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         = (\left[\mathbf{\Pi}_{0}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{D}\right]_{2}^{\mathsf{T}}) + (\left[\mathbf{t}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{z}_{0}\right]_{2}^{\mathsf{T}})
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                22 check \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{B}_1 \end{bmatrix}_1 \bullet \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{C}_1 \end{bmatrix}_2
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                               = (\left[\mathbf{\Pi}_{1}^{\mathsf{L}}\right]_{1}^{\mathsf{L}} \bullet \left[\mathbf{D}\right]_{2}^{\mathsf{T}}) + (\left[\mathbf{t}\right]_{1}^{\mathsf{L}} \bullet \left[\mathbf{z}_{1}\right]_{2}^{\mathsf{T}})
23 return 1
  11 \left[\mathbf{\Pi}_{j}\right]_{1} := \left[\mathbf{B}_{j}\right]_{1} \cdot \mathbf{S}_{j}
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                               \frac{\mathbf{Alg}\ \mathsf{PSim}(\mathsf{crs},\mathsf{td},\left[\mathbf{t}\right]_1):}{24\ \mathbf{parse}\ \mathsf{td}=:\mathbf{u}}
  12 \left[\mathbf{C}_{1-j}\right]_2 := \mathbf{S}_{1-j} \cdot \left[\mathbf{D}\right]_2^{\mathsf{T}}
 13 \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{\Pi}_{1-j} \end{bmatrix}_1^2 := \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{B}_{1-j} \end{bmatrix}_1 \cdot \mathbf{S}_{1-j} - \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{t} \end{bmatrix}_1 \cdot \mathbf{v}^{\mathsf{T}} 24 parse to =: u

25 \mathbf{v} \leftrightarrow \mathbb{Z}_{\mathsf{p}}^k

   15 return \pi

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                32 \left[\mathbf{\Pi}_{1}\right]_{1}^{2} := \left[\mathbf{B}_{1}\right]_{1}^{1} \cdot \mathbf{S}_{1}^{2} - \left[\mathbf{t}\right]_{1} \cdot (\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{v})^{\mathsf{T}}
33 \pi := \left(\left[\mathbf{z}_{0}\right]_{2}, \left(\left[\mathbf{C}_{i}\right]_{2}, \left[\mathbf{\Pi}_{i}\right]_{1}\right)_{i \in \{0,1\}}\right)
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  34 return \pi
```

<span id="page-51-0"></span>**Fig. 15.** NIZK argument for the OR language  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathbf{B}_0,\mathbf{B}_1}^{\vee}$ .

where

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{core}}_{\mathcal{B}_2}(\lambda) := & \left(4k\lceil \log Q \rceil + 2\right) \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{MDDH}}_{\mathbb{G}_1, \mathcal{D}_{2k,k}, \mathcal{B}_3}(\lambda) \\ & + \left(2\lceil \log Q \rceil + 2\right) \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ZK}}_{\mathsf{PS}, \mathcal{B}_4}(\lambda) \\ & + \lceil \log Q \rceil \cdot \Delta_{\mathcal{D}_{2k,k}} + \frac{4\lceil \log Q \rceil + 2}{p-1} + \frac{\lceil \log Q \rceil Q}{p} \right). \end{split}$$

We define  $\mathbf{u}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{2k}$  as a vector with all entries equal to zero except for the *i*-th entry, which is 1. Let  $\delta \in \{0,1\}$  and  $\mathsf{hv} = \mathsf{H}(\underline{\mathbf{A}}^\star, \underline{\mathsf{x}}^\star, \mathsf{t}) \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ . To construct the special simulation-sound tLM-NIZK proof system, we establish the following relations:

$$\begin{split} &-\left[\mathbf{A}_{i}\right]_{1}\left[\bar{\mathbf{w}}\right]-\left[\mathbf{x}\right]_{1}=\left[0\right]_{\mathsf{T}} &$$

{52}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-52-0"></span>
$$- \text{ For } j \in [2k], \ \left[\mathbf{t}^{\mathsf{T}}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\left[\delta\mathbf{u}_{j}\right]_{2}\right] = \left[\left[\delta\mathbf{t}^{\mathsf{T}}\right]_{1}\right] \bullet \left[\left[\delta\mathbf{u}_{j}\right]_{2}\right] \qquad \qquad /\!\!/ \quad \delta \in \{0, 1\}$$

$$- \left[\left[\mathbf{s}^{\mathsf{T}}\right]_{1}\right] \bullet \left[\mathbf{B}\right]_{2} = \left[\left[\delta\mathbf{t}^{\mathsf{T}}\right]_{1}\right] \bullet \left[\left(\mathbf{K}_{0} + \mathsf{hv}\mathbf{K}_{1}\right)\mathbf{B}\right]_{2} \qquad \qquad /\!\!/ \quad \mathsf{Ver}(\mathsf{svk}, \mathsf{hv}, \sigma) = 1$$

To provide intuition for the last two verification equations, consider the goal of enabling the simulation of a proof that demonstrates knowledge of a signature for an honest user. As noted in [GS08], the Groth-Sahai proof for non-homogeneous bilinear equations requires modifications to facilitate simulation. Specifically, we introduce a binary variable  $\delta \in \{0,1\}$  and its corresponding variable  $\delta \mathbf{t}$  to restore homogeneity to the equation fourth. To ensure the well-formedness of the commitment to  $\delta \mathbf{t}$ , we incorporate the third equation. However, the quadratic nature of the third statement necessitates 2k distinct equations to complete the proof.

Analogous to the one-time special simulation sound tLM-NIZK proof system, we employ  $\mathbf{z_A}$  to prove the first statement and  $\mathbf{z_\sigma}$  to prove the last three, ensuring that  $\mathbf{z_A} + \mathbf{z_\sigma} = \mathbf{z} \notin \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{D})$ . This condition enforces that either the first equation or the last four equations have perfect soundness. More specifically, we need to give the commitments  $[\mathbf{C_w}]_2$ ,  $[\mathbf{C_k}]_2$ ,  $[\mathbf{C_t}]_2$ ,  $[\mathbf{C_s}]_2$  and the proofs  $[\mathbf{P_A}]_1$ ,  $[\mathbf{P_d}]_1$ ,  $[\mathbf{P_x}]_1$ ,  $\{[\mathbf{P_t^j}]_1$ ,  $[\mathbf{Q_t^j}]_2\}_{j \in [2k]}$ ,  $[\mathbf{P_\sigma}]_2$  verifying the following equations:

$$- \left[\mathbf{A}_{i}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{w}}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} - \left[\mathbf{x}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{z}_{\mathbf{A}}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} = \left[\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{A}}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{D}_{2}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} \\ - \left(\left[\underline{\mathbf{A}}_{i}^{\intercal}\right]_{1} - \left[\left(\underline{\mathbf{A}}^{\star}\right)^{\intercal}\right]_{1}\right) \bullet \left[\mathbf{z}_{\sigma}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} - \left[\bar{\mathbf{A}}^{\intercal}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{k}}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} = \left[\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{d}}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{D}_{2}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} \\ - \left(\left[\underline{\mathbf{x}}^{\intercal}\right]_{1} - \left[\left(\underline{\mathbf{x}}^{\star}\right)^{\intercal}\right]_{1}\right) \bullet \left[\mathbf{z}_{\sigma}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} - \left[\bar{\mathbf{x}}^{\intercal}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{k}}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} = \left[\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{x}}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{D}_{2}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} \\ - \operatorname{For} j \in \left[2k\right]: \\ \left[\mathbf{y}_{1}\mathbf{t}^{\intercal}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{u}_{j}\mathbf{z}_{\sigma}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} - \left[\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{t}}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{u}_{j}\mathbf{z}_{\sigma}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} = \left[\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{t}}^{j}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{D}_{2}^{\intercal}\right]_{2} + \left[\mathbf{U}_{1}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{Q}_{\mathbf{t}}^{j}\right]_{2} \\ - \left[\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{s}}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{B}\right]_{2} - \left[\mathbf{C}_{\mathbf{t}}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\left(\mathbf{K}_{0} + \mathsf{hv}\mathbf{K}_{1}\right)\mathbf{B}\right]_{2} = \left[\mathbf{U}_{1}\right]_{1} \bullet \left[\mathbf{Q}_{\sigma}\right]_{2}$$

The construction of the special simulation-sound tLM-NIZK proof system is presented in Figure 16. This proof system achieves almost tight security under the  $\mathcal{D}_k$ -MDDH assumption. Additionally, the detailed simulator  $\mathcal{S} = (\mathsf{SSetup}, \mathsf{Sim})$  for the proof system  $\Pi_\mathsf{U}$  is provided in Figure 17.

The correctness of the proof system  $\Pi_U$  is straightforward. We provide the tight proof for the zero-knowledge property Theorem 9 and special simulation soundness of the proof system  $\Pi_U$  in Theorem 10. The detailed proof of the theorems can be found in F.

<span id="page-52-1"></span>**Theorem 9.** The tLM-NIZK proof system in Figure 16 is zero-knowledge. Concretely, for all PPT adversaries A, there exists an PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  such that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ZK}}_{\Pi_{\mathsf{U}},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq 2\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{MDDH}}_{\mathcal{D}_k,\mathsf{GGen},\mathcal{B}}(\lambda).$$

<span id="page-52-2"></span>**Theorem 10.** The tLM-NIZK proof system in Figure 16 is special simulation sound. Concretely, for all PPT adversaries A, there exists an PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  such that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{SSS}}_{\Pi_{\mathsf{U}},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{UF-CMA}}_{\mathsf{SIG},\mathcal{B}}(\lambda).$$

## F Proof of tLM-NIZK

#### F.1 Proof of Theorem 6

**Theorem 6.** The proof system  $\Pi_{OT}$  in Figure 11 is zero-knowledge. Concretely, for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists an PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  such that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ZK}}_{\Pi_{\mathsf{OT}},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq 2\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{KerMDH}}_{\mathcal{D}_k,\mathsf{GGen},\mathcal{B}}(\lambda).$$
